Abstract\n\nApproximately 85,000 liquid rocket engine failure reports, obtained from\n30 years of developing and delivering major pump feed engines, were reviewed\nand screened and reduced to 1771. These were categorized into 16 different\nfailure modes. Failure propagation diagrams were established. The state\nof the art of engine condition monitoring for in-flight sensors and betweenflight inspection technology was determined. For the 16 failure modes, the\npotential measurands and diagnostic requirements were identified, assessed\nand ranked. Eight areas have been identified requiring advanced technology\ndevelopment \xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n17. Key Words (Suggested by Author(s) I\n\nRocket\nRocket\nRocket\nEngine\n\n18. which have resulted\nfrom\n1.\n2.\n\nThe delivery of about 2500 engines\n\n3.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe development of eight different pump-fed liquid rocket engines\nThe launch of over 1000 flight vehicles\n\nover the last 30 years. These engine failure reports were reviewed,\nscreened, categorized and were reduced to 16 failure modes and failure propagation diagrams, which were common to all engines .\nA survey of the state of the art of sensors for in-flight and inspection\ntechniques for between-flight engine condition moinitoring was performed.\nThe in-flight sensors and the between-flight inspection techniques were\nassessed, matched, then ranked relative to their suitability for prognosis\nand diagnosis of the identified 16 failure modes. The highest ranked technology selections for both in-flight and between-flight were considered\nupgradable and the effort required to develop these technologies has been\nidentified.\nThe eight technologies that are potentially applicable to rocket engines\nare:\n1.\n\n2.\n\nFiberoptic deflectometer for bearing condition\n\n3.\n\nIsotope wear detector for wear particles\n\n4.\n\nTunable di9de laser spectrometer for wear particles\n\n5.\n\nUltrasonic flowmeter for propellant flows\n\n6.\n\nUltrasonic thermometer for high temperatures\n\n7.\n\nHolographic leak detector for fluid leaks\n\n8.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nOptical pyrometer for turbine blade temperature\n\nScanning pyrometer for blocked fluid passages\n\nINTRODUCTION\nFuture space transportation systems for low earth orbit must rely on reusable subsystems and routine ground 6perations to be cost effective. This\ncan be achieved by avoiding high costs associated with maintenance on a\nbasis other than for cause, and avoiding disassembly for routine inspection\nand premature component replacement. The approach to achieving substantial\noperations cost reductions by increasing rocket engine service life and\nreducing maintenance and turn-around time between flights is to incorporate engine condition monitoring. Engine condition monitoring includes\nboth in-flight condition monitoring and between-flight inspection. This\nstudy was conducted for the purpose of identifying technology advancements\nin engine condition monitoring needed to minimize liquid rocket engine\nmaintenance.\n\n.,\n\nThere has been a long history of development activity directed toward aircraft air breathing engine monitoring systems. Several Air Force aircraft/\nengine systems as well as engine-alone systems have been implemented\nrecently through prototype and operational applications. These systems\nhave been directed toward reducing propulsion support costs and improving\naircraft operational availability. Similar activity has existed with commercial airlines. However, prior to the advent of the Space Shuttle and\nthe Space Shuttl~ Main Engines, no large requirement for reusable liquid\nrocket engines existed. The Space Shuttle is bringing about new\nrequirements.\nThis study was undertaken to identify needed technology advancements in\nengine conditioning monitoring. The efforts (1) reviewed past rocket\nengine failures modes, (2) identified state-of-the-art technology for\nin-flight engine condition monitoring sensors and between-flight inspection\ntechniques to detect incipient component failures, and (3) identified areas\nwhere advancement in monitoring and inspectipn technology is required.\nThe study was performed in four tasks:\nTask I\n\n- Review and Characterization of Past and Present Rocket\nEngine Failures\n\nTaEik II\n\n- Identification and Evaluation of In..,.Flight Condition\nMonitoring Sensors\n\nTask III - Identification and Evaluation of Between-Flight Inspection\nTechniques\nTask IV\n\n- Eight Technologies Recommended for Additional Development\nEffort\n\nThe study was performed during part of 1980 and 1981.\n\n2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDISCUSSION\nCHARACTERIZATION OF ROCKET ENGINE FAILURES\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe objectives of Task I of this study were to identify engine failures of\nmain propulsion booster and space engines. regardless of propellant combination, to categorize these failures, and to investigate and evaluate the\nfailure modes in order to conduct an assessment of state-of-the-art technology of the in-flight engine condition, monitoring equipment and inspection\ntechniques \xe2\x80\xa2\nTo perform this task it was necessary to draw upon the Rocketdyne Reliability\nData bank for applicable failures, to categorize the data in some meaningful\nway, to reduce it to a manageable size, and to unravel the propagation of\nthe applicable failure modes to assist the investigation of the monitoring\ntechniques.\nThese data were to be submitted in an agreed-upon format that would simplify\nthe performance of the subsequent tasks of the study, and would record the\nresults.\n\nFAILURE ANALYSIS PROCEDURE\n1.\n\nDefinitions\n\nSince Rocketdyne has had a relatively long and rich experience in rocket\nengine development, testing and production (Fig. 1), it was decided to review\nand evaluate the reports associated with engine failures that could provide\na basis for the study.\nThe failure data accumulated at Rocketdyne over the years was estimated to\nbe in the neighborhood of 100.000 pieces of information. It became apparent\nthat some ground rules and screening were required to handle the mass of data\nin some consistent manner to obtain meaningful results to support the study.\nThe first decision was to select a definition for failure which would be\nconsistent with the approach. The definition is:\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n"Failure is the inability of equipment to satisfy performance\nor design specifications once the equipment has experienced\nsuccessful operation or acceptance or has the expectation of\nsuccessful performance without adjustment or rework:"\nThis definition permits the reporting of failures, which have been noted\nduring operation, as well as the reporting of conditions which would result\nin a failure if operation were permitted.\n\n3\n\neo\n\n66\n\nI\n\nTHO"\n\n86\nI\n\n70\n\n76\n\nI\n\nI\n\nATLAS\nI\n\nH\xc2\xb71\n\nI\n\nF\xc2\xb7\'\n\nJ\xc2\xb72\n\nI\nI\nI\nI\n\nI\nI\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\nI\n\nI\nI\nI\nI\nI\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nNO. OF\nENGINES\n\nI\n\nI\n\n15\n\n10\n\n124\n\n:.::-.:.:- -]\n\n1110\n\n214\n\n16\n1&3\n\n;\nI\n\n.\n\nRS\xc2\xb727\n\nI\nI\n\nSSME\n\nI\n\n---I . ----I\n:::3\nI\nI\nI\n\n!\n\niTA,\n\neo\n10\n\n2245\n\nA80UT 70% OF TOTAL USA\nItUMIt FED L1aUID "OCKET ENGINES\n\nFigure 1.\n\nRocketdyne--30 Years of Delivering Engines\n\nThe definition would become a first screen of the data by eliminating the\ntrouble reports in the system which are generated as a result of rejection\nof hardware due to improper paperwork~ cosmetic discrepancies (scratches on\npaint~ lack of torque stripe. etc.).\nWhile the boundaries of the failure\nwere determined~ the criticality of the failure had also to be defined.\nSince the format selected for presentation of the data required assigning\ncriticality factors~ these were defined as:\nCategory 1\nCategory 2\n\nloss of mission (includes both post-launch\nabort and launch delay sufficient to cause\nmission scrub)\n\nCategory 3\n2.\n\nloss of life or vehicle\n\nall others\n\nRaw Data Base\n\nBefore examining the criteria for subsequent screens~ it is helpful to\ndescribe the life cycle of an engine at Rocketdyne and in the field~ up\nto the point where the engine is expended, and to establish at which point\nfailure reports, or Unsatisfactory Condition Reports (UCRs) originate.\n\n4\n\nFigure 2 shows the typical activity to which the hardware, which constitutes\nan engine, is exposed. Separate pieces of hardware are received and tested\nprior to assembly in subsystems or assemblies in Receiving Inspection operations by the Quality Assurance organization. Once a component or parts\nthereof are deemed acceptable, they are ready for assembly into larger\ncomponents, subsystems, systems and finally into a complex system. The\nengine UCRs are written only when the component, having once demonstrated\nits ability to function according to specification requirements, fails to\nmeet these requirements.\nComponents are functionally checked during assembly and subsystems are\nfurther tested. Turbopumps are calibrated and assembled, and subjected to\na so-called "green run" on the component test stand. The green run is the\ninitial hot fire test of the assembled turbopump, verifying its ability to\ndeliver the desired performance. The turbine receives its working fluid\nfrom a slave gas generator: while the fuel pump delivers fuel, the LOX side\npumps water. After successfully passing the green run tests, the assembled\nturbopump is returned to the shop to be mounted on the engine. The thrust\nchamber and injector are also calibrated separately, in water tests, to\ndetermine the Delta-P and are then ready for assembly. For many years the\ngas generator, the component that delivers working gas to the turbine, was\ntested separately to determine its performance.\n\n*\n\n::~~~R:;I~~,n\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\n* l\n\n\':\';\':\':\'\':\'\'\';\'\';~~EL~E~M~E~N~T--------------:C~OMPO--N-E-N-T------ --EN-G-:-IN~E-- ------:--:VEHICLE\nPUMP\n.... IlFOllMANCE\n\n~\n\nCAlIallATED\nI\'UMI\'\nASSEMaLV\n"GIlEEN IlUN"\nT" ASIEMaLv\nDElIVEIlED\nTO ENGINE\n\n..\n\nGAS GENEIlATOll\n\nHOT FIIlE\n\nA"EMaLV\n\nVEHICLE\nACCEPTANCE STATIC\nHOT FIIlE\nFIllING\n\nVEHICLE\nLAUNCH\n\nACCEPTANCE\nHOT FIIlE\n\nCHAMaEIl\nJACKETDP\n\nDElIVEIlED\nTO VEHICLE\n\nTHIlUIT CHAMaEIl\nDELIVEIlED TO ENGINE\n\nINJECTOll DP\n\nFigure 2.\n\nGas Generator Cycle Rocket Engine Test Activity\n\n5\n\nAll these components, together with the thrust mounts, valves, controls,\nlines and ducts, electrical components and harnesses are assembled into\nan engine. UCRs are always written when a component does not meet any\nrequirements. Finally, the completed engine is subjected to a series of\nelectromechanical and leak checks, usually called the first E&M check.\nAfter passing this series of tests, the engine is mounted on a test stand\nand subjected to a minimum of two hot fire tests to verify engine performance and operation. Any nonconformance is written up as an UCR. After\ncompleting the hot fire, or acceptance tests, the production engine is\nreturned to the shop for another series of leak and electromechanical tests,\nthe second E&M check. After successfully passing these tests, the engine is\ndelivered to the customer. At this point, the engine is transported to\nanother contractor, where it passes receiving inspection tests, is installed\nin the vehicle and subjected to a new series of electrical, mechanical and\nleak checks. During this time, any discrepancy is written up as an UCR.\nSubsequently, the integrated vehicle is transported to the launch site, the\npayload is installed and the engine goes through the final series of checks\nprior to countdown sometimes including static firing. Further UCRs may be\ngenerated during this time, until, in conventional rocket engines, the\nengine is expended in launch.\nAs it can be seen, the Rocketdyne failure reporting system is designed to\nrecord on UCRs nonconforming conditions at various stages of the engine\nlife. In addition, the UCR provides disposition for the discrepant hardware; it outlines corrective action against future similar occurrences and\nsupplies trend data. Figure 3 shows two UCRs from the SSME data file,\nindicating all the information that is recorded regarding the discrepancy.\nAt this point, the selection of the engine systems, from which the UCR data\nwere going to be evaluated, was also made. Based on the study requirements,\nit was decided to use failure data from large liquid rocket engines; that\nis, systems that utilize pumps for propellant feed, rather than being fed\nfrom pressurized tanks. The engine systems selected are based on similarity\nof engine operation and of component configuration with the hypothetical\nreusable rocket engine.\nThe information retrived from the computerized Reliability Data Repository\nwas limited to current engine systems still in production as well as those\nthat had been designed for manned application in the Apollo program. Data\nfrom discontinued pump-fed engines, such as the Navajo, Jupiter and Redstone,\nwere not used since they are too far removed from the current concept of\nrocket engines. The selected engine systems are:\n1.\n\n2.\n3.\n\n4.\n5.\n6.\n7.\n\nSSME used in the Orbiter Vehicle\nJ-2 used in the. Saturn Ib and V Vehicles\nH-l used in the Saturn Ib Vehicle\nF-l used in the Saturn V Vehicle\nRS-27 used in the Delta Vehicle\nThor used in the Thor Vehicle\nAtlas used in the Atlas, Atlas-Centaur Vehicles\n\nThese systems are described briefly in Appendix A.\n\n6\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n". ISf t l11.\n\n\'":Jl/\n\nU-lJ-1I\n\nFigure 3.\n\nDUO\n\nSample of UCR (SSME Oxidizer Main Valve)\n\n7\n\nAfter excluding other data from programs that did not fit the initial\ncriteria, that is UCRs originating in programs related to the engine systems\nlisted above, the data base consisted of some 84,000 pieces of information.\n\n3.\n\nData Screening\n\nConcentrating on the objective of the study, it was desirable to use only\nUCRs which could provide the basis for investigation of rocket engine\nsensors. Since the UCR search covered an extensive time period during the\ndevelopment stages and the production of these engine systems, it was\ndesirable to concentrate on the failures originating during the operational\nphase of the engine.\nScreen No.1. The criteria to eliminate failure reports of components and\nengines of experimental configuration were established. The most costeffective way was to retain the data originating from production and flight\nconfiguration engines. This allowed an automatic sort of the data stored\non computer tapes. The criteria for sorting the failure data were by engine\nserial number denoting production/flight engine systems as follows:\nSSME\n\n- All 2xxx series plus flight configuration engines 0006,\n0008, 0009 .\n\nAtlas - All llxxxx and 22xxxx series engines\nRS-27 - All OOxx series\nF-l\nJ-2\n\nAll 4 digit series\n\nH-l\nThor\n\n- All 6 digit series\n\nA further screening of the Atlas and H-l engine data removed information\nrelated to earlier models as unsuitable for the analysis.\nFigure 4, indicates the phase from which the failure data was drawn, relating\nit to reliability growth. Because data from mature engine was desirable,\nUCRs from the early life of the program were excluded.\n\n>-\n\n!::::I:\n:1a:l3:\n\nPRODUCTION AND\nFLIGHT PHASE\nFAILURE DATA\nSELECTED FOR\nEVALUATION\n\n\xc2\xab0\n-a:\nu:!(!)\n\na:\n\nTIME\n\nFigure 4.\n\n8\n\nFailure Data Selection\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nScreen No.2. The next screening step was to select failure data from hot\nfire tests, flight/launch operations and post hot fire tests. Each production engine, before being delivered, is subjected to one or more hot fire\ntests to determine its operational characteristics and to verify the integrity of the system.\nThese tests, performed at the Santa Susana Field Laboratory (SSFL) test\nstands (F-l testing was performed at Edwards Air Force Base facility) and\ncalled acceptance tests, were found most suitable for the study. The\nacceptance tests dupli~ate, as much as possible, the operation of the\nengine systems during launch and flight with the exception of duration and\nacceleration.\nScreen No.3. Another screening step resulted from elimination of the failure reports due to causes that would obscure the goal of the investigation.\nThe following categories were excluded as unsuitable:\n1.\n2.\n3.\n4.\n5.\n6.\n7.\n8.\n\nProcedural problems\nHuman error\nFacility and vehicle discrepancies\nLow frequency failures (one-time occurrences)\nExperimental hardware or procedures\nSecondary failures\nObsolete hardware\nInformation type instrumentation failures\n\nScreen No.4. In addition, because the bulk of the failure data thus\nobtained originated from the expendable rocket engine experience, the VCRs\nwere screened with respect to their impact on reusability. Also, where\ndesign information exists, the life of the component was compared to its\ndesign life. In addition, conditions that could have been monitored to\ndetect the incipient failure were listed.\nFigure 5 shows the original data base that was available for the study and\nthe reduction in numbers after the successive screening operations.\n4.\n\nData Base After Screening\n\nFigure 6 shows the distribution among the several engine systems of the\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n1771 VCRs that were left after screening. It is not surprising to note\nthat over three-fourths of the retained VCRs corne from engine systems that\nrepresent 857. of the delivered engines.\n\n9\n\nENGINE\nSYSTEM\n\nINITIAL\nNO. Each failure mode\nrepresents an event during which the resnective engine system failed to perform according to specifications.\nEach component failure mode was evaluated and the following was determined:\n1.\n\nIf the failure was predictable or unpredictable\n\n2.\n\nIf the failure would be detectable in flight, on the ground, or\nnot at all\n\n3.\n\nIf the failure was functional or operational\n\n4.\n\nIf the failure was primary or secondary\n\n5.\n\nIf the failure caused performance degradation or was catastrophic.\n\nIn addition, where design information exists, the life of the component was\ncompared to its design life. Conditions that could have been monitored to\ndetect the incipient failures are also listed. The successive screenings\nof the UCRs written against the failures of the matured applicable engine\nsystems have reduced the number of pieces of information from over 84,000\nto 1771.\nThe UCRs that passed all successive screening steps were grouped by engine\nsystems prior to making an individual assessment. Within each engine system,\nthe UCRs were analyzed and the failure was evaluated with the aid of drawings,\nschematics, exploded views and test data, when available. In addition, to\nassist in understanding the failure mechanism, an analysis of the sequence of\nevents leading to the incident was made using a graphic illustration. This\nis later described in Failure Propagation Block Diagrams, and shown in Fig. 8.\nBased on the assessment, the UCRs were grouped in failure modes. The operation was completed separately for each of the engine systems selected for the\nstudy. The resulting failure modes were then compared and integrated. This\neffort resulted in 16 modes for 1771 UCRs.\nAll information collected from the failure reports was thus included in the\nforms, called summary sheets, that are submitted in Appendix B. A brief\ndescription of each failure mode is presented below.\nlAo\n\nBolt Torque Relaxation\n\nA main oxidizer valve which controls the flow of propellant to the main com-\n\nbustion chamber, caught fire during engine operation, requiring premature\nengine shutdown. Investigation of the failure disclosed that a screw that\nsecures one of the internal seals of the valve had become loose as a result\nof cavitation and vibration, and allowed fretting of aluminum assemblies in\na liquid oxygen environment.\n\n11\n\nf-\'\n\ntv\nENGltlE\n\n\'"EOUfIlCY\n\n2.\n\nLIFE\n\n..\n\n0<\npESlGHJ-eTUAL\nFAILURE.\n\nt_lant ".~".,e leeka!!\n\nYUle" ., .. terlal ._r I tt Ie\n\nMe"\'\n\n011\nR(X)NO.....V\n\'AILUR\xc2\xa3\n\nOvert......n4\nI \xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2ute\xc2\xb7\n\n\'rh.. ry\n\n\xc2\xa3\':t\xe2\x80\xa2 ..,..1 fuel lult.,.\n\n"\n\n2.211\n\n~\n\n,..,MARY\n\n....ILUIIE TYPE\n\nL.ss ., fuel at "\'zzl.\ntullts , ....... ,re..ture\nen, I ftC cut.\' f II. te "\'OT\nfill ""-rwe t.-...erature\nexcee"in, re411ne.\n\n..\n\n....ILUIIE alIODE .....D C"\'USE\n\nNazzl. Di~IUI\nS.A.W \xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2 D ,lul\nUltrlSanlc TIIe.-tor (Fl ... )\n#\nUlt\'lSanlc Fl_tor I_nle)\n\'-I,..-ter\nT.... ~lo DIM LlSO\' S,..,t..-tor (Mixture btl.)\n\n\'FrWEEN .lIGHT IIlISPECTION TECHNIQUES\n\nIlEM""ICS/COIIlMENTS\n\nUltrlse_lc L..t\nAcaustlc Hal.,r.....\'\nI-r., 1114f.,r.,hy\n" - III_i.,..,hy\n\',...toxl_e \'-I\xe2\x80\xa2 ...,..\'hy\n~,..,,...\n\n\'-Io,..,r.""y\n\nH~ter\n\nQ,tlcol ,,......try\nHal.,..,hlc Loot\nIIfllt. .ter-wo... I_terfe.-try\n\n-\n\nFigure 7.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n,.t\n\nSample Failure Summary\n\n,\n\n.\n\n\'\n\n.\n\n~\n\nr+\nCAVITATION\n\nVI ...ATION\n\n-\n\n....EMATU..E\nCUTOFF\n(VI ...ATION\nSAFETV)\n\n~\n\nL+\n\nACOUSTICS\n\n.\n\n-\n\nCO.... E\nEX"LOSION\n\nSample - Failure Propagation Block Diagram\n\nThe rubbing of the two metal parts caused heat and subsequent fire t and\nextensive damage. The valve was redesigned to eliminate this failure\nmode.\nlB.\n\nBolt Torgue Relaxation\n\nIn another engine system, the same failure mode t bolt torque relaxation,\ncaused the loosening of a seal retainer in the sequence valve of the main\noxidizer valve t as a result of excessive vibration due to high flow velocity\nand/or engine vibration. This t in turn t allowed the pressurant gas (helium)\nto escape prematurely to the gas generator control valve open port, actuating this valve out of sequence. Premature operation of the gas generator\ncontrol valve caused a detonation in the combustor with damage to the\nassembly.\n2.\n\nCoolant Passage Splits\n\nThe thrust chamber assembly, common to all engine systems t is the component\nthat transforms the energy stored in the fuel into kinetic energy. This\nfluid is contained and directed by the sides of the thrust chamber. During\nengine operation, the thrust chamber is exposed to high pressure, to vibration, to high temperature all in a brief period of time. The strength and\nthe cooling capability of the thrust chamber is achieved through an\ningenious design.\n\n13\n\nContainment is accomplished by a series of circular bands that ring the\nthrust chamber, the lightness by using thin walls, and the cooling by\nrecirculating fuel. The requirement of lightweight and high heat transfer\ncapability is achieved by constructing the thrust chamber walls of tubes\nthrough which the fuel circulates. High thermal strains, stresses induced\nby vibration, surges during the ignition and transition stages, containment\nwithin the tubes causing obstructions and material deficiencies are some\nof the causes of failure of the cooling passages. Loss of coolant through\nthe thrust chamber walls may cause loss in engine performance and loss of\ncooling capability which will lead to engine failure.\n3.\n\nJoint Leakage\n\nAll liquid propellant engine systems suffer from leakages from the interfaces of the propellant and pressurant fluid ducting. Defects in material,\nimproper installation causing damage to seals or sealing surfaces, warping\nor distortion of sealing surfaces due to thermal strains during engine start\nor operation, fastener torque relaxation during engine operation are causes\nof this failure mode. Effects on this failure mode vary depending upon the\nlocation and the type of leakage, and some of them have had catastrophic\nconsequences.\n4.\n\nHot Gas Manifold Transfer Tube Cracks\n\nThis failure mode is peculiar to one engine, which utilizes double-walled\nducts to convey hydrogen-rich hot gases from the preburners to the high\npressure fuel turbopumps. Excessive high temperature transients have caused\nhot spots or cracks on the inner wall (liner) which may evolve into a complete failure of the component with catastrophic effect.\n5.\n\nHigh Torque\n\nHigh torque, as a result of rubbing the labyrinth seal in propellant pumps\nhas been experienced in several engine systems. The seal consists of a\nseries of land and grooves designed to minimize leakage from the high pressure side of the pump to the low pressure inlet side. Excessive temperature\nand vibration can lead to friction between the static and rotating parts\nthus increasing the torque of the turbopump with eventual subsequent\nfailure.\n6.\n\nCracked Turbine Blades\n\nGenerally, turbine blades are subjected during start and main stage operation to high energy transients which could be due to pressure, temperature,\nor accoustical spikes leading to failure as a result of localized heating\nof turbine parts. Impact on turbine blades of debris and contaminants in\nthe hot gases has also caused damage, with resultant loss of efficiency\nand imbalance of the turbine.\n\n14\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n7.\n\nFailure of Bellows\n\nFlexible ducting is used to convey propellants between some components to\navoid problems that afflict rigid ducting. The bellows are damaged by highcycle fatigue, which is caused by high energy transients and high flow\nvelocity. The effects of this mode of failure vary depending on the location of the duct, as well as on the type of fluid conveyed through the\nbellows.\n8.\n\nLoose Electrical Connectors\n\nSome engines are more dependent on electrical controls than the previous\ngeneration of rocket engines, and have encountered instances where failures\nwere reported caused by incomplete electrical circuits. The failures were\ndue to connectors that loosened as a result of vibration from engine operation. The consequences of this mode of failure vary according to the\naffected electrical circuit.\n8.\n\nBearing Damage\n\nExcessive loading of bearings in the highly stressed turbomachinery may\nlead to wear-out and eventual failure. Contributing factors are excessive\naxial and radial loads, vibration, and friction, an4 the effects are generally\ncatastrophic if not detected in time.\n10.\n\nTube Fracture\n\nThe failure mode noted on one engine occurred on a particularly sensitive\ncomponent that caused premature engine operation cutoff, and was the result\nof vibration-induced fatigue.\n11.\n\nTurbopump Face Seal Leakage\n\nIn the engine systems that utilize turbopumps to convey the propellant under\npressure to the combustion chamber, it is imperative to prevent leakage along\nthe rotating shaft. The seal leakage is especially critical in the engine\nsystems that use a common shaft to power the fuel and the oxidizer pumps,\nbecause the mixing of propellant at that location has catastrophic consequences. The cause of the seal leakage is generally due to excessive\ntemperature gradients, vibration, friction, or interface material damage\nsince the seal has to prevent leakage in both a static and in a dynamic\ncondition.\n12.\n\nLube Pressure Anomalies\n\nSeveral anomalies in the lube (oil) system have been noted during this investigation, and all have been grouped in the same category as they affected primarily a subsystem peculiar to certain types of engine systems. The lubrication subsystem delivers oil under pressure to the turbopump gearcase for\nlubrication and cooling gears and bearings through jets and nozzles. The\nfailures included in this category consist mainly of obstruction of the flow\ndue to contamination.\n\n15\n\n13.\n\nValve Fails to Perform\n\nIn this category, failures of different valves were included. These failures\nwere mainly caused by contamination or excessive friction. The effects upon\nthe engine performance vary depending upon the function of the part.\n\n14.\n\nInternal Valve Leakage\n\nThis category comprises all those incidents in which an engine failure was due\nto internal leakage within valves. The several failure mechanisms which can\nlead to this condition ate so noted in the summary sheet.\n\n15.\n\nRegulator Discrepancies\n\nAll regulator failures were grouped in this categury since they pertain to\na subsystem that is used in a few of the engine systems included in this\nstudy. The function of the regulator is to reduce pneumatic supply pressure\nto a required level and to maintain it at that level throughout engine\noperation. Malfunction was caused mainly by contamination.\n\n16.\n\nContaminated Hydraulic Control Assembly\n\nThe failures that were included in this category are peculiar to one engine\nsystem. The hydraulic control assembly receives and directs hydraulic control pressure in the proper sequence for the operation of the engine main\nvalves during start and shutdown and controls also their position during\nmainstage operation.\nViewing the number of UCRs that fell into the different categories gives an\ninteresting picture of relative magnitude. The pie chart (Fig. 9) depicts\nthe distribution of UCRs by failure mode and verifies what was known from\nprevious experience; that the major problem that plagues liquid propellant\nrocket engines is leakage. Over three-fourths of UCRs are related to\nleakage, either internal to components, or external from joints. Thus the\ndependence on leak testing the engine systems at various stages of their\nlife, and the reluctance to break into a subsystem for minor reasons, disturbing proven joints, is justified.\nAll summary sheets for the 16 failure modes are presented in Appendix B.\nRevision to Failure Modes Listing\nFurther evaluation of the 16 failure modes indicated that some should be\ndropped from the analysis. Consequently, failure modes 1 and 4 (bolt torque\nrelaxation, and hot gas manifold transfer tube cracks) were excluded because\nthe corrective action taken in both cases was redesign. Thus, under previously established ground rules they would have been eliminated as not meeting the mature engine definition. They had escaped the screening because the\nredesign occurred on flight engines, which were by definition considered\nto be mature.\n\n16\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFigure 9.\n\nDistribution of UCRs Among Failure Mod~s\n\nFailure Mode 12, Lube system anomalies, was also deleted because it is not\napplicable to a reusable engine.\nIt should also be noted that the Failure Mode and Effect Analysis of every\nengine system under consideration indicates other possible failure modes in\naddition to the 16 that were determined for this study. These additional\nfailure modes have not been experienced in testing the mature engines,\ntherefore, it has been established that further investigation was not\nwarranted for an occurrence that has an extremely low frequency.\nIn conclusion, the screening process has been effective in reducing a large\namount of failure data to a manageable number by following a series of logical steps saving only that information that could contribute significantly\nto the study. It also showed that most failure modes are common within the\nvarious engine systems. This gives more confidence in selecting these\noccurrences for determination of suitable in-flight condition monitoring\ndevices and between flight inspection methods and equipment.\nFAILURE PROPAGATION BLOCK DIAGRAMS\nTo support the study\' for the applicable in-flight condition monitoring devices,\na method for depicting failure modes was provided to indicate the sequence of\ncontributing events.\n\n17\n\nThese events, usually an anomalous system performance, show the relationship\nbetween a symptom which could be monitored and the eventual failure of the\nengine system to perform. The analysis method was to attempt to slice the\nperiod of time in which the failure develops into small increments and survey the changes that occur. Isolating the contributing factors in time\nassisted in the selection of suitable sensors. As depicted in Fig. 10, the\nevents are shown as rectangles, and the sequence, left to right, indicates\npassage of time.\nThe failure propagation block diagrams included in this report (Appendix C)\nare typical for each failure mode listed and were not repeated for each\ndifferent engine system, which may have a similar mode for slightly different components.\nFlight Failures\nTo complete the failure investigation of the concerned engine systems, it was\ndeemeq necessary to examine also the flight failures caused by these systems\nover the years, since these occurrences were not covered by VCRs but by\nspecial reports. This examination resulted in preparation of failure propagation block diagrams similar to -those discussed in the previous pages. These\ncharts do not indicate sensing devices, since most of the flights were\nboosted by engine systems developed for military use, which carried limited\ninstrumentation. The theory under which these engine systems were designed\nand tested was to get the vehicle off the launch pad whether it was functioning properly or not. The engines were generally devoid of monitoring\ndevices and the shutdown controls were inactive before a predetermined operating duration, the alternative being the destruct button.\n\nr\n\nr+\nCAVITATION\n\nVI.PlATION\n\n....\n~\n\nI--<\n\n4\n\nACOUSTICS\n\n.\n\nCORRECTIVE ACTION:\n1. PlEDESION\nLVALVE\nb. SCPlEW\n\nFigure 10.\n\n18\n\n\'PleMATUPle\nCUTOFF\n(VI.PlATION\nSAFETYI\n\nl-\n\nPlETAININO\nSCPlEW\nLOOSENING\n\n~\n\nFPlETTING\n\n~\n\nSample Failure Propagation Block Diagram\n\nFIPlE\nEX\'LOSION\n\nIt can be clearly seen that the charts illustrating the failures indicate\npossible points in the propagation of the failure where detection of the\nincipient failure could have limited damage to the engine and to the vehicle.\nFAILURE DATA CONCLUDING REMARKS\nThe analysis and evaluation of the 1771 failure reports screened from the\ntens of thousands of VCRs in the Reliability Data Repository resulted in a\nsummary presented in Fig. 11 and accounts for only 16 failure modes.\nExamination of the data surveyed during the performance of Task I did not\nreveal any surprises, except that the expected number of failure modes\nencountered in 30 years of testing and flying rocket engines was rather\nsmall.\nAs expected, the Atlas engine system, which consists of 3 separate engines,\nexhibited most failure modes - eleven.\nAnother feature that appeared during this investigation is the commonality\nof failure modes among the various engine systems. That is not surprising,\nsince the similarity in configuration between most of the engines. The\nfailure modes that appeared only on one engine system are those that occurred\non a component peculiar to one system (like hot gas manifold transfer tube\ncracks, or Hydraulic Control Assembly) or because the engine system is more\ndependent than others for successful operation on proper functioning of the\ncomponent (Failure Mode 8, Loose Electrical Connectors).\n\nFAILUftE\nMODE\nCATEGOftY\n1\n\n2\n3\n\nENGINE SYSTEM\nFAILUftE MODE DESCftlPTION\n\nSSME\n\n10LT TOftOUE ftELAXATION:\nA MAIN OXIDIZEft VALVE\nI SEOUENCE VALVE\nCOOLANT PASSAGE LEAKAGE\n\n34\n\nJOINT LEAKAGE:\nA. Several sequential\nstudies were conducted to this goal: A comprehensive literature search and\nreview generated a list of novel, state-of-the-art (SOTA) and conventional\nsensors (Appendix E). Correlation with potential measurands applicable to\nthe previously experienced failures, plus some practical considerations,\npared this list to a manageable, relevant level for deeper analysis\n(Appendix F).\nThe comparison of competing technologies was evaluated with a screening\nsystem (Appendix G). The selected technologies were compared for technical,\neconomical and temporal factors which yielded a final list of ranked failuredetection technologies.\nSURVEY\nThe literature survey established a baseline for existing state-of-the-art\nand novel technology used for condition monitoring On in-flight systems.\nThe survey revealed those sensors and monitoring systems used most frequently\nfor diagnostic and prognostic purposes. The survey was well rounded in that\nit covered industrial processes, ground transportation, and the electronics\nfield, as well as aircraft and aerospace. From the surveyed 89 articles,\n20 novel and 14 state-of-the-art sensors were found that may be applicable\nto on-board rocket-engine condition monitoring. These sensors are listed\nin Table 1, along with the sensors already used in in-flight rocket-engine\napplications.\nINTRODUCTION\nA portion of the Task I I effort was devoted to conducting a literature\nsearch for in-flight condition-monitoring technologies that would be applicable to a reusable rocket engine. The survey for condition-monitoring\nsystems covered the fields of aircraft and aerospace, transportation,\nindustrial processes, the medical industry and electronics. The results\nof the literature search, including uncovered novel and state-of-the-art\ncondition monitoring devices, are presented in Appendix E.\nFAILURE MONITORING SENSOR ASSESSMENT\nThe potential condition-monitoring detection technologies were to be\nexamined to determine their suitability for detection of the 16 failure\nmodes obtained from Task I. This was achieved by first transforming the\nfailure modes into the measurands that could potentially be detected by\nsensors. To obtain all the measurands of each failure mode, the failure\nmode was analyzed to determine the stages leading to its incidence and\nidentify corresponding measurands capable of detecting each stage of the\nfailure.\n\n20\n\nTABLE 1.\n\nCOMMONLY USED IN\nROCKET ENGINES\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRESISTIVE TEMPERATURE\nDETECTOR\n\xe2\x80\xa2 STRAIN\xc2\xb7GAGE PRESSURE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MAGNETIC It was with the help of this distinction that it was possible\nto apply the speed (of a few milliseconds) screen to the indirect technologies, to detect the process transients. In contrast, the speed screen\nwas not applicable to direct technologies. For example, relatively slow\npressure build up in a contained joint t indicating a slow leak t is best\nsensed directly by a pressure sensor. Fast response time for this trendingtype observation is not significant.\nUpon thorough review of various screens only four distinct screens were\ndetermined to be unequivocal. Two of these screens were valid on~y for\nindirect condition-monitoring technologies; namely speed and failsafeness.\nThe other two were applicable only to the direct condition-monitoring\ntechnologies, i.e\' t bulkiness and numerosity.\nFor indirect technologies it is necessary to capture the frequency and\namplitude of transients of process (f10w t pressure t temperature t rpm)\nmeasurements. In rocket engines these transients are typically a few\nmilliseconds in duration. By totalizing the transients it may be possible\nto determine the maximum stress exposures t which provides information\nregarding the remaining life of the component. ConventionallYt however t\nprocess sensors are designed with damping to generate an average signal.\nThis eases contro1lin~ of the process measurand.\nThe second indirect screen is failsafeness t which implies no catastrophic\nhazard to the engine if the measuring device malfunctions. With the aid\nof these four go-no-go screens, the 33 direct condition-monitoring technologies were reduced to 12 and the 33 indirect technologies were reduced\nto seven as shown in Tables 4 and S.\nUpon examination of the acceptably screened direct-diagnostic sensors,\nfive devices were recognized as possessing well established limitations\nregarding their rocket engine applicability. To preclude carrying these\nwell-known conventional instruments any further in ranking t their utility\nwas termined. The five sensors t comprised of strain gage and piezoresistive\naccelerometers and nicke1 t semiconductor and the thermocouple thermometers,\nare denoted by a deletion sign in Table 6 resulting in only 12 direct\ncondition-monitoring technologies remained for grading and ranking.\nIn a11 t only 19 techno1ogies t consisting of 11 novel, 6 state-of-the-art\nand 2 rocketry were acceptable for further grading and ranking.\n\nGRADING AND RANKING\nUpon successful application of the four screens t 19 technologies remained\nto be graded and ranked.\nA consistent and methodic rationale was needed to grade all these technologies. Thus, the economic liabilities consist of the expenditure to develop and integrate the technology\nand the economic virtues represent the return on the investment in terms of\ninspection-labor saving and hazard detection and prevention. The technical\nvirtues on the other hand, consist of elements called lumped descriptors\nwhich describe the ability of the technology to detect accurately, correctly,\nconstantly and safely .\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nThe salient descriptors of each of these lumped descriptors were selected.\nThe result is shown in Table 7. It should be noted that the signal-conditioning and data-processing requirements are included under the electronic\ngroup of technical liabilities and are not considered separately elsewhere.\nSubsequently, quantitative values (scores) were assigned to each lumped\ndescriptor based on a consistant grading scale.\nOne additional dimension was added to the comparative ratings. This factor,\ndevelopment time, is related to a program schedule which can influence the\nviability of the technology according to the need and application determined by specific programs.\n\nTABLE 7.\n\nTECHNICAL AND FINANCIAL DESCRIPTOR CATEGORIES AND DESCRIPTORS\n\nTECHNICAL REQUIREMENTS\n\nTECHNICAL PERFORMANCE\n\nEXPENDITURES\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 PHYSICAL\nWEIGHT\nSPACE\nSTRENGTH\nMATERIAL\nCHEMICALS\nRESONANCE\nFATIGUE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDETECTIBILITY\nSPEED\nACCURACY\nREPEATABILITY\nSENSITIVITY\nRESOLUTION\nDRIFT\nARTIFACTS\nSUSCEPTI BI LITY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDURABILITY\nRECALIBRATION\nINSPECTION\nLIFE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSAFETY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nELECTRONIC\nPOWER. CONSUMPTION\nVOLTAGE\nCURRENT\nWIRING\nFILTERING\nAMPLIFICATION\nANALOG/D IGITAL\nMEMORY REQUIREMENTS\nSIGNAL CONDITIONING\nLI N IZATI ON\nEAR\nSHIELDING\nFUNCTIONAL\nINTRUSIVE\nPOWER\n\nR&D\nINTEGRATION\n\nFAIL SAFETY\nFAILURE EFFECTS\n\n29\n\nUsing a 0 through 10 relative scale for each lumped descriptor, the detection technologies for each failure mode were numerically graded. To be as\nobjective as possible, each technology was graded according to its own\nfeatures, independent of its utility or need criticality. The functional,\ndetectabi1ity,and safety categories were considered more important, hence\nwere assigned a twofold weighting factor relative to the physical, electronic and durability categories; the rationale being that if you cannot\nmeasur~ the failure without burdening and hazarding the rocket engine, it\ndoes not matter how small, durable or electronically demanding the detection technology is. An example of each such rating is shown in Table 8.\nThis table is divided into direct and indirect condition monitoring groups\nwhich are henceforth ranked independently. Indirect condition monitoring,\nto detect a failure, requires extensive data processing to correlate engine\noperational parameters under varying loads, rpm, temperatures, pressures.\nDirect condition monitoring, in contrast, requires very little data processing because it monitors the condition of the component independent of the\npropellant temperature, pressure, or flow.\nIn all, nine similar tables were completed and are included as Appendix G.\nIt is noted that, of the original 16 failure modes, not all were developed\ninto technology rankings. Three modes were eliminated and four were not\ndetectable through any viable in-flight condition-monitoring means.\nNext, the technical, financial, and development-time ranks of each technology were added together yielding the overall grade for the technology.\nThe technical rank was weighted significantly higher than the other ranks:\nthe rationale was based on the fact that any technology, regardless of\nhow well developed it is for non-rocket industry application, still requires\na significant amount of testing, adaptation, and modification efforts and\nexpenditures before it can be flown in a rocket engine.\nThe outcome of the detection technology grading and the corresponding rankings is summarized in Table 9. Table 10 identifies the ultrasonic thermometer and flowmeter as to the two top-ranking, most promising indirect condition-monitoring technologies, followed by digital quartz pressure sensor\nand optical tachometer. These four technologies combined could indirectly\ndetect eight generic failure modes, but they require extensive in-flight\nengine-parameter correlation, trending, thresholding, totalizing, data\nprocessing, etc. The same table identifies pyrometer, fiber-optic deflectometer, isotope wear detector and tunable diode-laser spectrometer as the\nmost promising direct condition-monitoring technologies capable of in-flight\ndetection of all nine failure modes.\nCONCLUDING REMARKS\nIn summary, the computerized literature search, from an on-line six-millioncitation data bank yielded a review of 289 abstracts and 78 articles. From\nthis review, 20 novel and 14 state of the art in-flight condition-monitoring technologies were identified.\n\n30\n\nTABLE 8. L.1J\n,...... - l\n\nl.J.J\n0::::\n::l\n-l\n\n......\n\nc(\n\nl.L.\n\nLJ.J\n\n:>\n\n-l\ne(\n\n:>\nI\n\n~\n\ne(\nLJ.J\n...J\n\n(/)\n\nO::::LU\n\n-l\ne(\nZ\n0::::\nLJ.J\n\nIZ\n\n0 ......\nI- U\n\ne(Z\n-le(\n::l e..\n(!J l.J.J\nLU 0::::\n\n...... o::::u\nI\n\n(V\')\n-=::t\n,...... ,......\n\nI\n\n(/)\n\nLO ......\n,...... Cl\n\nDIRECT\nFIBEROPTIC DEFLECTOMETER\nPYROMETER\nTURNABLE DIODE-LASER SPECTROMETER\nISOTOPE WEAR DETECTOR\n\n~\n\n@\n\n@\n\n79-\n\n82\n79\n\n79\n82\n79\n\n79\nI@ 82 ~\n75 79\n78\n\n~\n\'-.:./\n\n83\n\n@\n\n@\n\n,~\n\n78\n\nINDIRECT\nULTRASONIC THERMOMETER\nOPTICAL TACHOMETER\nULTRASONIC FLOWMETER\nDIGITAL QUARTZ PRESSURE SENSOR\n\n@\n81\n\n76\n\n78\n\n78\n\n78\n\n.\n\n33\n\nNext, the 16 failure modes and failure propagation diagrams were analyzed,\nresulting in 23 distinct in-flight failure-detecting measurands. These\nmeasurands were then correlated with novel, state of the art, rocket-engine\nand conventional technologies resulting in 33 direct and 33 indirect\npotential condition-monitoring technologies.\nA selection approach was applied successfully using four nonequivocal\nscreens and several lumped descriptors. The screening process rejected\ninapplicable technologies. The lumped descriptors were employed for\ngrading arid ranking of the remaining 19 applicable in-flight conditionmonitoring technologies.\nThe ranking was achieved by assigning relative numerical grades to each\ndevice feature.\nSince these technologies vary in their state of maturity\nand utility, they were graded and ranked assuming they are completely\ndeveloped and are used for only one failure mode at a time.\nSuch an approach resulted in identifying four top-ranking direct conditionmonitoring devices capable of detection of all failure modes and four topranking indirect condition-monitoring devices capable of detection of eight\nout of nine failure modes:\nThe direct condition-monitoring devices are:\n1.\n\nPyrometer detects rotating-blade temperature\n\n2.\n\nIsotope wear detector detects bearing, rotary-seal\nand valve-seat wear.\n\n3.\n\nFiberoptic deflectometer detects bearing loading and\ndeflection\n\n4.\n\nTunable diode-laser Spectrometer detects nonmetal wear\n\nThe indirect condition-monitoring devices are:\n1.\n\nUltrasonic thermometer\n\n2.\n\nUltrasonic flowmeter\n\n3.\n\nDigital quartz pressure sensor\n\n4.\n\nFiberoptic tachometer\n\nIt should be noted here that other technologies such as Raman spectroscopy,\nferromagnetic torquemetering and exo-electron detection, although eliminated\nby this screening and grading process, have unique condition-monitoring\ncapabilities and should be carefully followed for any major breakthrough\nwhich could render them applicable to in-flight condition-monitoring.\n\n34\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nBETWEEN-FLIGHT INSPECTION\nThis task determined the between-flight inspection requirements that would\nprovide engine component reverification and remaining life assessment for\nthose failure-prone components identified in Task I. The applicability of\nbetween-flight inspection technologies and their implications on engine\ndesign and operation were evaluated with respect to those requirements.\nThe upgrading or development required of each technology was also identified.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n..\n\nAn approach similar to the method used in Task II was taken. A survey was\nperformed to identify existing inspection technologies which might be\napplicable to rocket engines. This included inspection procedures which\nhave been in routine use for many years as well as experimental techniques\nused soley in a laboratory environment. The results of Task I were then\nexamined to identify the between-flight-detectible measurands associated\nwith each failure mode. This led to the development of general inspection\nrequirements and their correlation with the surveyed technologies. The\ntechniques corresponding to each failure mode were evaluated on an equal\nbasis of development, resulting in scores which ranked the technologies\nand became inputs to Task IV. Accessibility requirements, engine configuration modifications and estimates of the effects on engine reliability\nand safety were determined for each inspection and included in the scoring.\nINSPECTION TECHNOLOGY SURVEY\nA survey was undertaken to find inspection technology which could be\napplicable to reusable rocket engines between flights. This survey\nincluded computer literature searches, periodical reviews, and personal\nvisits. Representative literature was enumerated and the inspection\ntechniques uncovered were then summarized (Fig. 13).\n\nCOMI\'UTEII SEAIICHES\n\xe2\x80\xa2 IIOCKWELL TIl\'S\n}\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LOCKHEEO DIALOG\n\xe2\x80\xa2 NASA IIECON\n\nL1TEIIATUIIE\nIIEFEIIENCES AND\nIIILIOOIIA\'HIES\n\n1.000.000\nCITATIONS\n\n\'EIIIOOICALS\n\n1\n14.\nAISTIIACTS\n\n!\n.1\nAIITICLES\n\n30\n\nSTATE-DF\xc2\xb7THE\xc2\xb7AIIT\nTECHNOlOOIES\n\nFigure 13.\n\nII\nNOVEL\nTECHNOlOGIEI\n\nLiterature Survey Utilized Multiple Resources\nto Uncover Inspection Technologies\n35\n\nIt should be noted that the purpose of these searches was to provide a\nbroad survey of between-flight engine condition monitoring technology, but\nnot a complete bibliography of this subject. Documents were selected for\nenumeration if they contained different technology, applications, or\napproaches than had been previously encountered. Also, literature\ncovering extensively used technology, or technology clearly not applicable\nto rocket engines was not chosen for examination.\nResources\nFour computerized searches were made, producing a total of 945 listings.\nThe search parameters were purposely left rather general so that technologies which have only seen limited or specialized use might be identified.\nThree of the searches looked for literature dealing with inspection technology for aerospace engines as well as various basic inspection concepts.\nThe fourth search dealt with leak detection only. Each listing was examined to determine if the defined document might contain new and useful\ninputs to Task III. Literature which might pertain to the Task II effort\nwas also identified. A brief description of each search follows.\nThe Rockwell TIPS search (Fig. 14) was an on-line examination of the combined database of five Rockwell International Divisions (Rocketdyne, Space\nSystems Group, Science Center, and North American Aviation, both Columbus\nand Los Angeles Divisions).\nTwo searches were conducted on Lockheed\'s DIALOG, a combined database of\nthe NTIS, Engineering Index, Inc., and Data Courier, Inc. (Fig. 15): a\nsearch of leak detection technology and a more general inspection technology search.\nThe NASA RECON computer search yielded a total of 267 citations (Fig. 16),\nbut because of duplications of citations in the Rockwell TIPS and Lockheed\nDIALOG searches, only 15 of these documents were selected for review.\nBeyond the computer searches, discussions were held with Air Force personnel about their diagnostic and nondestructive (NDI) programs.\nAdditional information was obtained by reviewing the references of literature located by the computer searches and by examining recent periodicals\nfor articles concerning nondestructive inspection methods.\nSurvey Results\nLiterature that was found to contain useful information was tabulated as\nshown in Table 11 and Appendix H. Information pertaining to in-flight\ndiagnostics was forwarded to the Task II effort.\nThe number of different inspection techniques described in each document,\nas indicated in Table 11, have been divided into three categories. Rocket\nEngine refers to technology which is or has been successfully applied to\nliquid propellant rocket engine inspection. Novel refers to any other\ntechnology, ranging from the conceptual stage of development to having\nseen only limited success as a maintenance facility technique. The\ntechniques uncovered through the survey are listed in Table 12 for each\ncategory. Table 13 gives a brief summary of each technique along with\ntypical uses, advantages and limitations. Most of the techniques might,\nwith development, be usable in situ; meaning with the engine installed\nin the vehicle. These in situ techniques are of significant interest\nbecause of the savings in turnaround time afforded with no requirements\nfor engine removal.\n\nTABLE 12.\n\nINSPECTION TECHNOLOGIES LOCATED BY\nLITERATURE SURVEY\n\nROCKET ENGINE\n\nSTAH\xc2\xb7OF THE ART\n\nNOVEL\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nULTRASONIC EXTENSIOMETRY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 ULTRASONIC LEAK DEl\'ECTION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nACOUSTIC flOLOGHf,PHY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nULTRASONIC FLAW DETECTION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nACOUSTIC EMISSION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSCANNING ACUU,s,IC FLUW\nOETECTION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nX\xc2\xb7RAY RADIOGRAPHY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI)AIHICLE RADIOGRAPHY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nISOTOPE HtE.FIMOMf.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nGAMMA\xc2\xb7RAY RAOIOGRAPHY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nfl.UOROSCOPY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nISOTOI\'E TRACER Dl.TLCIION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MAGNETIC PARTICLE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMAGNETIC PlRTURBATlON\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nREMNANT MAGNf.: f1ZATION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 PENlTRANT DETECHON\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 BARKHAUSEN NOISE ANAL YSIS\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 PENTOXIDE POLAROGflAI)HV\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 CONNECTOR CONTINUITY CHECKING\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 PARTICLE ANAL Y!SIS\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 HYDROGEN POLAROGFIAPHY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 HYGROMETRY\n\xe2\x80\xa2 FLOW I.EAI< DETECTION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 OllllCAL LEAK DETECTION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LEAK TAPl/COAlING\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDIFFl:RENTIAL RADIOMETRY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nELlIPSOMETliV\nHOLOGRAPHIC MAPPING\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 L.ASER IN1UIHBOMlIIIV\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SCANNING OPTICAL PVI-lOMllllY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMASS SPEC\xc2\xb7rROMfTRY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 THERMAL CONDUCTIVITY LEAK\nCHECKING\n\nmy\n\nLASER SUBFAC[ SCAlf[ 1l111H.i\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 TORQUING\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 OPTICAL. The measurands associated\nwith each failure mode were determined first as listed in Table 14. The\nmeasurands are shown in Fig. 17 and Appendix C, along with the propogation of the failure to give a clear indication of where they become\ndetectible.\n\nTABLE 14.\n\nINSPECTABLE FAILURE MODES AND MEASURANDS tDENTIFIED\n\n2 COOLANT PASSAGE LEAKAGE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 METAL EMBRITTLEMENT\n\xe2\x80\xa2 RESTRICTION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 TUBE SPLITS\n3 JOINT LEAKAGE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 WARPING DISTORTION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 TORQU~ RELAXATION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LEAK\n5 HIGH TURBOPUMP TORQUE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 PHYSICAL INTERFERENCE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 EXCESSIVE TEMPERATURE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 EXCESSIVE FRICTION\n6 CRACKED TURBINE BLADES\n\xe2\x80\xa2 HIGH TEMPERATURE TRANSIENT\n\xe2\x80\xa2 FOREIGN OBJECT DAMAGE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 FATIGUE\n7 CRACKED CONVOLUTION, BELLOWS SHIELD\n\xe2\x80\xa2 HIGH TEMPERATURE TRANSIENT\n\xe2\x80\xa2 FOREIGN OBJECT DAMAGE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 FATIGUE\n8 LOOSE ELECTRICAL CONNECTOR\n\xe2\x80\xa2 HIGH TEMPERATURE TRANSIENT\n\xe2\x80\xa2 TORQUE RELAXATION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 CONTINUITY\n9 BALL BEARING DAMAGE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 EXCESSIVE TEMPERATURE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 EXCESSIVE FRICTION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 WEAR\n\n10 TUBE FRACTURE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LINE DEFLECTION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 FATIGUE\n11 TURBOPUMP SEAL LEAKAGE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 PHYSICAL INTERFERENCE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 EXCESSIVE TEMPERATURE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 EXCESSIVE FRICTION\n13 VALVE FAILURE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MOISTURE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 INTERNAL FRACTURE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 GALLING\n\xe2\x80\xa2 CONTAMINATION\n\n14 INTERNAL VALVE LEAKAGE\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFRETTING\nCONTAMINATION\nTORQUE RELAXATION\nDISTORTION\nSTUCK COMPONENTS\nLEAKAGE\n\n15 REGULATOR DISCREPANCIES\n\xe2\x80\xa2 CONTAMINATION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LEAKAGE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 EXCESSIVE FRICTION\n16 CONTAMINATED HYDRAULIC CONTROL\n\xe2\x80\xa2 CONTAMINATION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LEAKAGE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 EXCESSIVE FRICTION\n\n47\n\nMETAL\nEMIIUTTLE\xc2\xb7\nMENT\n\nSAMI"LE: FAILURE MODE 2\n\nTU\'E\nS.. DEFORM...TION\n\nFigure 17.\n\nTask I Propagation Diagrams Reviewed to Determine\nDiagnostic Measurands\n\nCorrelation of Surveyed Techniques With Diagnostic Requirements\nAfter locating between-flight inspection technology with the literature\nsurvey and defining diagnostic requirements based on the characterization\nof past failures. the inspection techniques were matched to applicable\nfailure mode measurands. This correlation is shown in Table 15. The\nmeasurands are listed across the top. grouped by failure mode and the\ninspection techniques are listed at the left, grouped by detection type.\nEach possible inspection is indicated by an N. S. or R, depending on\nwhether the use of that technique could be considered novel, state of the\nart, or rocket engine state of the art for that particular failure type.\nThis table gives a singular summary of the multiplicity of use of each\ninspection technique, the number of techniques available toward each\nfailure mode and the present level of detectibility of each failure type.\nDESIGN AND INSPECTION COMPATIBILITY ASSESSMENT\nAccessibility and engine configuration modification requirements to utilize\neach inspection technique were identified and included in the technology\ngrading. In addition, engine configuration modifications which could\nprovide enhanced inspection capability were identified and assumed in the\ngrading.\n\n48\n\nTABLE 15.\n\nBETWEEN-FLIGHT FAILURE MODE DETECTION TECHNOLOGY\n\nH \xe2\x80\xa2 NOVEL\nTECt+\xc2\xablLOQ,Y\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 \xe2\x80\xa2 SUTE Of\nTHE ART\n\nTECHNOLOGY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 \xe2\x80\xa2 ItOCKET\nTECHNOLOGY\n\nN 0\n\nI I I\nI I\n\n\'I\nII\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI I I\n\ns\n\nI I\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI\n\nII\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI I II\n\no\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI I\n\nN\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 \xe2\x80\xa2\n\n!\n\nI\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\'.,\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI I\n\nI I\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\n010\n\n010\n\nI\n\n:5 I!IS I i l\n\nis\n\nI\n\nI\n\nIN\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\n\'.,\n\nI\n\nI\n\nIII\nI\n\nI I\nI\nI\nS\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI,\n\n,s I\n\n"\n"\n\nI\nI\nI I\n\n,\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nN\n\n~S\n\ni\n\nII\n\nI\n\nI IS I\n\nI\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nN\n\nSI\n\nIS\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 I\n\n"\n\nI\n\n,\n\nIN\n\nI\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\no\n\ni.\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nj\n\n0\n\nS\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI I\nI\nSIS I\n\nI\n\n01\nI\n\n0\n\n0\n\no\no\n\nI\n\no\n\nSOLUTION\nfiE DECAV\n\xc2\xb7fAJLUJIlE )JaDE: O.VIATEO 8V IM""OVEO DESIGN\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 \xc2\xb7TECHNIOUE NOT Al-fIIUCA.LE TO ANY FAILUl\'te MODES\n\n+\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n+TECHNIOUE NOT DIAGNOSTIC BECAUSE CDU"ONENT DISASSEU8LY IS "EQUINED\n\n49\n\nI\n\nAccessibility Requirements\nThe accessibility requirements were determined for each inspection technique as applied to each failure mode. It was found that six types of accesibility could be defined, in approximately decreasing desirability, as\nfollows:\nA\n\n- Direct External Access: where no interference problems with\nother components would normally be encountered.\n\nB\n\n- External Access with Interference: where considerations\nregarding the engine configuration, such as duct routing,\nwould usually be required of the design in order to provide\nadequate inspection accessibility.\n\nC\n\n- Internal Port Access: where an inspection port (or flight\ninstrumentation sensor) would have to be removed to provide\naccess to the interior or a component.\n\nD\n\n- Component Removal Access: where the removal of a component\nwould be necessary to provide adequate internal access for\nthe inspection.\n\nE\n\n- Component Disassembly Access: where, after removal, a\ncomponent would require major disassembly to accomplish the\ninspection.\n\nF\n\n- Component Addition Access: where the addition of an on-board\ncomponent would be required in order to carry out the ground\ninspection.\n\nTable 16 indicates the accessibility requirements, using the above accessibility codes, in the same format as was used in the technology/failure\nmode correlation matrix (Table 15). This information became an input to\nthe technology grading, affecting the engine application, hazard, integration, and development descriptors as appropriate.\nConfiguration Modifications\nAlthough most of the inspection techniques would impact on engine design\nconfiguration, few would affect it in a manner inconsistent with typical\ndesign requirements. Access ports and component removal needs are considerations normally encountered and, with prudent design practices, will\nhave minimal impact on engine weight or performance. Access to components\nfor inspection has not usually been a problem in regard to preventing\nfailures.\nThe lack of adequately-developed (for rocket engine use)\ninspection or prediction technology has been the major hinderance. There\nare two engine sub-components, however, that have been typically difficult\nto apply state-lf-the-art rocket engine inspection technology to. Both\nare subcomponents of a turbopump, a high speed precision machine which\noperates under extreme environmental conditions.\n\n50\n\nTABLE 16.\n\nBETWEEN-FLIGHT INSPECTION ACCESSIBILITY\n\n.l..i.Wit!\nEXTEfIlNAL ACCESS CODES\n\nA. , DIREC\' ACCESS\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nHiTE\'H"E~[NCr\n\nWITH\n\nOTHER COtM"ONENTS\nJNTEftNAL ACCESS CODES\n\nC\n\nJ\n\n\'ORT ftEQUJlltEO\n"ENOVAL\nfIlEOU(RED\nCO~PONENT O!SASSEMBLY PlEoutftEO\n\no : COMPONENl\nE ;\n\nF\' c COM\'ON[NT AOOlTIOh\nftEQU 1PI: EO\n\nBETWEEN-FLIGHT\nINSPECTION TECHNIQUES\n\n;;\n\n@\n\n:;\n\n,\n\nI\n~\n\nI\n\n, ...\n\nI\n\n...\n\n0\n\nI\nI\n0 0\n\n\'I\'\niii\n\nI:\n\n,\n\nI\n\nI\n\n! :\n\nE\n\nIC\n\nr\n\n,.\n\n,I\n\nI\n\n,;\n\nI\n\nII:\n\nI\n\nJE\n\nI\n\n,\n\nr\n\nI\n\nI;\n\n,\n\nI\n\n"\n\nI\n\nCI\n\n,\n\nI\n\n\'E\n\nr "\n\nI II\'\n\n1\'1\n\nt!\'\n\nIII\n\nIe\'\n\n"I\n\ni\nI\n\nI).\n\nI\n\n,\n\nI\n\n"\n\n)\'\n\nd,\n\nI\n\n,\n\n, I ,,\n,\n\n,\n\nI\n\nI\n\n:C!\n\nI\n\n; I\n\nI\n,\n\nI\n\nI\'\n\nil!\n\nE\n\nr\n\n,\n\n,\n\nI\n\nI\n\nIe I"\n\n;\nI\n\nI\n\n\'I.\n\nI!I\n\nI\n\nC\n\nI\n;\n\n,\n\nI\n\n\'e\n\nI\n\n,\n,,\n\nI\n\n,,\n\nI\n\n,\n\nC;\n\n,\n\nII,\n\nE \'IISIS\n\nI\n\nI\n\n\'I.\n\njl\n\nI\n\nI\n\n;\n\n;\\.\n\n:i\'\n\n0\n\nIr,\n\n1;:-\n\n"\n\nt\n\ni:i\n\nI\n\nI\n\n,\nI!\n\n.\n\nI\n\n!\n\nI\n\nr\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nC\n\nI\n\nC\n\nI\n\ncc\n\nCC\n\n,...\n\n,\n\n. DECAY\n\xc2\xb7rAlLURE MOOE OIVIATED 8Y IM~RDVED DESIGN\n\xe2\x80\xa2 \xc2\xb7T\xc2\xa3CHNIQUE NOT\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n~L1CA8LE\n\nTO ANY rAILURE MODES\n\n+TECHNJQUE NOT DIAGNOSTIC .ECAUSE CO ..".HENT DJSASSE".lY IS "[QUI"EO\n\n51\n\nThe bearings, because of the manner in which they must be structurally\nsupported, have been difficult to inspect unless the turbopump is disassembled.\nIn-flight vibration monitoring has not been a reliable\nindication of bearing condition either. The diagnostic technology\nidentified by this study should alleviate this problem as well as provide\nmuch better failure prediction with only modest configuration considerations. The isotope tracer techniques require an in line device, sensor for\nin-flight monitoring or collector for between-flight analysis, which could\nbe external to the turbopump. The fiberoptic bearing detector requires\nonly fiberoptic access to the outside of the bearing outer race, a consideration which might be difficult to retrofit on an existing turbopump but\nwhich could be incorporated in a new design.\nAccess to turbopump turbine blades is typically difficult, especially for\nmultiple-stage turbines, often requiring removal of the turbopump from the\nengine.\nThese subcomponents operate at very high rotational speeds in a\nhot gas and thus are vulnerable to over-temperature conditions and fatigue\nlives which must be carefully monitored and predicted.\nImproved accessibility to the turbines through the use of removable turbine housings (not\nrequiring the removal of the entire turbopump) or some other means would be\nhighly desirable. This would enhance the use of a wide number of inspection\ntechnologies which are otherwise difficult to employ. The development of\nthis design feature would be significant but the benefits of great improvements in inspectability, life prediction and repair would be a major\nadvancement in the maintainability of reusable rocket engines.\nTECHNOLOGY SELECTION AND UPGRADING\nThe method for evaluation and ranking of the technologies was developed in\ncooperation with the Task II effort. The evaluation method selected was,\nas in Task II, a two-step approach. First, two clear-out screens were\nidentified and applied to the techniques, resulting in the elimination of\nsix technologies. Lumped descriptors, each made up of many specific descriptors, were then defined. The technologies applicable to each figure\nmode were graded using these lumped descriptors, thus providing a ranking\nof the techniques. All techniques were assumed to be equally developed\nfor use on rocket engines.\n\nBefore ranking the techniques, it was desirable to eliminate from further\nconsideration those technologies which were clearly not amenable to the\ngoals of this study. Although many criteria with which to screen out\nthese unacceptable techniques were considered, only two appeared to be\nunequivocal. They are:\n1.\n\n52\n\nNeed - Is the technique applicable to any of the Task I failure\nmodes? Although valuable diagnostic techniques which were\nidentified by the literature survey, three technologies, particle\nradiography, fluoroscopy, and ellipsometry were eliminated by\nthese screen.\n\n2.\n\nComponent Disassembly - Does the use of this technique necessitate\nmajor disassembly of a component? Such disassembly would defeat\nthe goals of on-condition maintenance diagnostics. Three additional techniques, magnetic particle, magnetic perturbation, and\nBarkhausen noise analysis were eliminated from consideration by\nthis screen. All three applied only to turbopump bearing\ninspection.\n\nTechnique Grading\nInasmuch as many specific descriptors were not available to evaluate the\ntechnologies, lumped descriptors were defined, each incorporating several\nspecific parameters. With this approach, errors in the judgments of\nunknown information tend to balance with both other missing and known\ndescriptors for a fair grade of each lumped category. This, in turn,\nreduces the possibility of biasing the overall ranking with a distorted\ngrade, appearing as a seemingly well-substantiated score, for anyone\ndescriptor.\nThe lumped descriptors were grouped as being either technical, economic,\nor developmental in nature. The technical and economic groups were subdivided so as to differentiate between the required inputs and resulting\neffects of using the technology. The lumped descriptors are:\nTechnical Requirements\nApplication\n\nThose requirements concerning the use of the inspection equipment, including accessibility, human\ninterfacing, and engine configurational needs.\nWeighted score to reflect the importance of these\nfactors.\n\nAuxiliary\n\nAuxiliary requirements involved in performing the\ninspection, including electrical, mechanical, and\ncomputational needs.\n\nPhysical\n\nPhysical characteristics of the inspection equipment,\nsuch as size, weight, complexity, material and\nchemical.\n\nTechnical Features\nDetectability\n\nHow well the technique can identify the failure.\nThis includes many factors such as accuracy, repeatability, sensitivity, resolution, drift, susceptability and level of failure progression. This is a\nkey descriptor and is weighted accordingly.\n\nDurability\n\nHow rugged or reusable the inspection equipment is,\nas well as how much maintenance such as recalibration\nis needed to maintain the required level of detection.\n\nSpeed\n\nThe time needed to perform the inspection, including\nequipment setup, use, removal, and data processing.\nSince a reduction in turnaround time is a major goal\nwith associated savings in operational costs, this\ndescriptor was given a weighted score.\n\n53\n\nHazard\n\nDanger of initiating a failure with use of technique,\nincluding the sensitivity to an improperly performed\ninspection.\n\nEconomic Expenditures\nR&D Costs\n\nThe estimated cost to upgrade or develop tbe technique for use on rocket engines.\n\nIntegration\nCosts\n\nApproximate cost of incorporating the use of the\ntechnique into an engine design after upgrading or\ndevelopment. Does not include equipment costs.\n\nEconomic Savings\nOperational\nSavings\n\nApproximate savings through reduced turnaround and\nlabor or improved predictability with the use of\nthe technique. Savings were assumed at $100.00 per\nhour for 1,000 inspections, i.e., a I-hour improvement would result in $100,000.00 in savings for\n1,000 inspections.\n\npeve10pment\nDevelopment\n\nThe number of years necessary to advance the technology to routine rocket engine use.\n\nThe technologies were graded as indicated in Table 17 and Appendix I for\neach failure mode.\nIn order to achieve technical scores which could be\nfairly compared, the technologies were assumed to be equally developed for\nuse on rocket engines. The scores given for technical lumped descriptors\nwere summed to give an overall technical score. Economic costs were subtracted from savings, resulting in an overall savings figure. An economic\ngrade was then assigned based on one point for each nearest $100,000 in\nsavings. The development grade was determined by subtracting one point,\nfrom a maximum of ten points, for each year required for development. A\ntotal overall grade was obtained by summing the technical, economic, and\ndevelopment grades.\nTECHNIQUE SELECTION\nThe results of the grading can be interpreted in many ways.\nIn all cases,\nhowever, it should be remembered that the grades are inherently subjective,\nwith disagreements over scores inevitable. For this reason, small differences between grades should be considered neither a decisive distinction\nnor an indication of nearly equal status. For the purpoBe of selecting\ntechnologies for further development, however, some ranking basis was\nneeded, so the total overall grades, as calculated, were employed.\n\n54\n\n,.\n\nTABLE 18 is a summary of the grading.\nIt is important to note that the\ngrades given are maximum and, in general, apply to only one failure mode.\nThis table does give a good overall technology ranking because, as can be\nseen by studying grading tables in Appendix I, a technique which may rank\nhighest in only one failure mode tends to rank high in others for which\nit applies; similarly, a low-ranking technique tends to rank low for all\napplicable failure modes.\nIn order to provide adequate detection or prediction for each failure type,\nthe top ranking inspection technique, based on its total overall grade, was\nselected for each failure mode. As Table 19 shows, seven technologies were\nchosen, three of which were highest for more than one failure mode. Two\nof the techniques, thermal conductivity leak detection and connector continuity checking, are considered rocket engine techniques; one, particle\nanalysis, is a stage-of-the-art technology; and four, holographic leak\ndetection, exo-electron emission, scanning optical pyrometry, and isotope\ntracer detection, are considered novel.\nWhen developed, these seven technologies could be used to detect or predict\nall of the failures identified by Task I. However, in some cases in-flight\ndetection may provide better or more timely detection and existing rocket\nengine inspections, although not highest ranking, may be adequate. These\nfactors were evaluated, with resulting recommendations, in Task IV.\nCONCLUDING REMARKS\nThe Task III effort paralleled the Task II approach. A literature survey,\nover a database of over six million citations, was performed and yielded\n56 tabulated documents. A review of these documents identified 14 rocket\nengine techniques, 16 state-of-the-art techniques in other industries and\n16 novel techniques. The results of the Task I failure characterization\nwere examined to identify between-flight measurands. The techniques\nidentified by the survey were then correlated to the applicable failure\nmode measurands to provide a matrix of inspection possibilities.\nA selection procedure was used to identify the top ranking inspection\ntechnique for each failure mode. First, six technologies were eliminated\nas being clearly inadequate. The remaining techniques were then graded\nusing lumped descriptors which included factors such as accessibility,\nengine configuration requirements, detectibility, speed, costs, savings\nand safety. The techniques were graded assuming an equal state of\nmaturity for each failure mode application. Based on the grades for\neach lumped descriptor, the technologies were assigned total overall\ngrades which permitted a ranking of the applicable inspection technology\nfor each failure mode.\nSeven technologies were identified as being topranking in one or more of the 16 failure modes. They are:\n1.\n2.\n\nThermal Conductivity Leak Detection - Leak detection in localized\nareas.\n\n3,\n56\n\nHolographic Leak Detection - Leak detection of multiple joints\n\nScanning Optical Pyrometry - Detection of coolant passage\nrestrictions or leaks.\n\nTABLE 18.\n\nGRADING OF BETWEEN-FLIGHT DIAGNOSTIC TECHNOLOGY\n\nN \xe2\x80\xa2 NOVEL\nTECHNOLDGY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 = STATE OF\nTHE
