Cl\n\nI Cl\n\n0.0\n\n>-\n\n,... :::I:\n\n@\n1.t64\'\n@ 1\\:/\n14\n\n=APPLICABLE TECHNOLOGY GRADES\n0= TOP-RANKING TECHNOLOGY FOR EACH FAILURE MODE\n\n\'I\n\n..J\n.....\n\n::E:t.!:l\n\nXX\n\n...\n\n~\n\n..J\na\n\n~\n\n>\n..J\nce\n>\n\n~\n\n62\n\n@\n\n4.\n\nExo-Electron Emission - Fatigue monitoring and characterization\n\n5.\n\nConnector Continuity Checking - Verification of electrical\nconnector function.\n\n6.\n\nIsotope Tracer Detection - Monitoring wear.\n\n7.\n\nParticle Analysis - Monitoring wear and contamination .\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n59\n\nFUTURE TECHNOLOGY DEVELOPMENT\nThe objectives of this task were to review the results from In-Flight Condition\nMonitoring (Task II) and Between-Flight Inspection (Task III) and to establish\nan objective prioritized list of technology development requirements.\nTECHNOLOGIES SELECTED FROM TASK II AND TASK III\nThe procedures followed in Task II and Task III were similar; a survey of\ntechnologies was made and matched with the failure modes identified in Task I,\nwhich were then ranked and these technologies became the inputs to this task\nand are:\nTask II:\n\nIn-Flight Condition Monitoring\n\nDirect In-Flight Sensors\n1.\n2.\n3.\n4.\n\nOptical pyrometer\nFiberoptic deflectometer\nIsotope wear detector\nTunable diode-laser spectrometer\n\nIndirect In-Flight Sensors\n1.\n2.\n3.\n4.\nTask III:\n1.\n2.\n3.\n4.\n5.\n6.\n7.\n\nUltrasonic flowmeter\nUltrasonic thermometer\nDigital quartz pressure sensor\nOptical tachometer\nBetween-Flight Inspection\nHolographic leak detection\nThermal conductivity leak checking\nScanning optical pyrometry\nExo-electron emission\nConnector continuity checking\nIsotope tracer detection\nParticle analysis\n\nAPPLICATION OF SELECTED TECHNOLOGIES TO ROCKET ENGINES\nIn~Flight\n\nCondition Monitoring\n\nConsiderable reductions in cost and time in maintaining a long service life\nfor reusable rocket engines can be realized by the use of in-flight condition\nmonitoring systems. The search and screening of such systems yielded eight\nstate of the art and novel technologies which can be used to detect symptons\nrelated to failure modes previously experienced in rocket engines. They\ninclude optical pyrometers, fiberoptic bearing deflection detectors, isotope\nwear detectors, tunable diode-laser spectrometers, ultrasonic flowmeters,\nultrasonic thermometers, digital quartz pressure sensors, and optical tachometers.\n\n60\n\nA description of each of these technologies is presented in a discussion of\nthe steps in development required for their integration into rocket engines.\nDirect In-Flight Sensors\n1.\n\n..\n\nOptical Pyrometer for Remote Temperature Monitoring of Turbine\nBlades\n\nTechnical Description. All objects emit a spectrum of thermal radiation\ncharacteristic of their emissivity and their temperature. An optical\npyrometer measures the frequencies and amplitudes of the spectrum of this\nthermal radiation. and thus provides a noncontacting. nonintrusive. fast\nmeans of temperature sensing. A system for temperature sensing by means of\na pyrometer is shown in Fig. 18. The pyrometer consists of a semiconductor\ndevice in which electromagnetic quanta are converted into electrical current.\nThe current magnitude is measured by an electronic system. In measuring\nthe temperature of an object. the thermal radiation is optically filtered by\na window into one or two narrow frequency bands to give a unique thermal\nfingerprint of the object. For observing the temperature of parts internal\nto a system. the filtered waves are transmitted to the pyrometer by means of\noptical fibers. allowing isolation of the detector and its electronics from\na hot. hostile environment.\n\n..\n."\n\n..\n\nTEMPERATURE\n\nINDICATDR\\\n\nOBJECT\nOF\nMEASUREMENT\n\nOPTICAL/\nFIBER\nCABLE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nDETECTDR7\n\nFigure 18.\n\n\\ELECTRONIC\nSIGNAL\nPROCESSING\n\nBlock Diagram of Pyrometer for Remote\nTemperature Measurement\n\n61\n\nFurther advantages of this pyrometer system for turbine blade measurement\ninclude the nonintrusiveness of the detector, preventing any hazard or aeration as well as its immunity to creep and fatigue, by virtue of its crystalline structure. Furthermore, a response time of 0.6 microseconds or less is\npossible, enabling transients to be accurately monitored. The versatility\nof today\'s electronics makes possible complete thermal profiling or imaging\nof a device, as well as strobed observation.\nAt the temperature range typical of rocket engine turbine blades (538 to\n807 C), accuracies as good as 0.5% can be achieved by these devices.\nPyrometers have, in fact, been used for remote in-flight monitoring of turbine blade temperature in airplanes.\nDevelopment Requirements. To be incorporated into a rocket engine, the\nfollowing steps in development are required. The measurement requirements\nfor the pyrometer must be defined in terms of its resolution, accuracy and\nrange of frequency sensitivity desired, and a choice of pyrometer made. The\nproper fiberoptic and window materials for the high-temperature environment\nof the turbine must be chosen. Finally, the electronics must be laid out,\nthe system integrated and a prototype fabricated and tested in the field.\n2.\n\nFiberoptic Bearing Deflection Detector\n\nTechnical Description. A fiberoptic deflection detector monitors the condition of a bearing by measuring the localized, cyclic deformations on the\nouter bearing race caused by the passage of the balls, by a detector mounted\nnear the race. The key element of this detector, as shown in Fig. 19, is a\nbundle of optical fibers with its end in close proximity to the outside\nradial surface of the outer race. Light is both shined onto the race and\nreflected from the race to a detector through this fiber bundle. The magnitude of the reflected photoelectric signal is a function of the variation of\ndistance between the race and the sensing element. \' The output of the probe\nfor a normal bearing is very similar to a clean half sine wave, corresponding\nto the passage of each ball by the probe. When the surface of one of the\nballs becomes pitted or flawed, the photoelectric output of the detector is\ncorrespondingly distorted each time that ball passes over the fiberoptic\nprobe, thus generating a distorted half sine wave once per revolution. On\nthe other hand, a crack or pit on the inner surfaces of the bearing races is\nnoted as a distortion in each of the output peaks, corresponding to the\npassage of each ball over the flaw in the race.\nFiberoptic bearing deflection detectors are well suited for bearing performance monitoring. The detector provides a noncontacting means of measuring\nbearing deflections and loads directly. High levels of sensitivity, such as\n50 mV/micron, are commonly achieved. By virtue of the fiberoptic coupling,\nthe device has a profile which facilitates installation and is immune to,\nelectromagnetic noise.\nDevelopment Requirements. Methods of mounting the detector should be evaluated which minimize the effect of structural vibrations on the output. The\ndetector electronic circuitry should be laid out, fabricated and tested. A\nfiberoptic material should be chosen which best functions in a cryogenic\nenvironment.\n62\n\nLIGHT SOU"CE 1==::::::::;3===::::;;:::~\n(L.E.D.)\nT"ANSMITTING\nO\'T1CAL\nFlftER\n\nI\'HOTO\nDETECTO"\n"ECEIVING\nOI\'TICAL\nFI8ERS\n\nOUTE" I\'lACE\nIALL IEAlUNG\n\nINNE" I\'lACE\n\nFigure 19.\n3.\n\nFiberoptic Deflectometer\n\nIsotope Wear Detectors\n\nTechnical Description. An isotope-wear detector measures the low-energy\nOSHA-approved gamma rays of wear particles of ball bearing or rotary seals.\nIt consists of a gamma-ray detector and a particle catcher downstream of the\nisotope-tagged bearings or seals. The y-photons emitted by the captured\nparticles strike the detector and are converted into an electric current.\nElectronic circuitry then measures the magnitude of this current, determining\nthe rate of y-photon emission and thus the quantity of wear particles.\nTagging is effectively accomplished by immersing the part in a flux of\nneutrons (e.g., by placing it in a storage hold of a nuclear reactor), converting a small, uniformly distributed fraction of its atoms into radioisotopes. The part is then incubated for several months, leaving it tagged\nwith only long-lived radioisotopes suitable for long-term monitoring of wear.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMaterials chosen for bearings because of desirable mechanical properties are\noften ferromagnetic (such as the steel bearings in the SSME turbopump), and\ndebris from parts made of such materials could be captured by a magnetic trap\nlocated on a duct at a bend in flow downstream of the part (Fig. 20). Centrifugal forces would move the debris radially outward at the bend into a\nbed of magnetic pins which capture these particles for measurement by a y-ray\ndetector. An alternative method, suitable for turbopump rotary seals, routes\nthe purging or cooling fluid passing through the seal through a duct to a\nlower-pressure region, where a filter with a low pressure drop captures a\nrepresentative amount of the wear particles.\n\n63\n\nFLOW OF ISOTOPE\xc2\xb7TAGGED\nBEARING\xc2\xb7WEAR \'ARTICLES\n\nIA\n\n.\n\n;\n.\n. ..\n\n~~-;:\n..\n~\n\n,\n\nI\n\n\'\n\nFLOW\n\no\n\n., :\n;\n\n.\n"\n\nFigure 20.\n\n;\n\n:\n\nMETAL SCREEN\n\n: .!/.JTRAPPED WEAR\n\n:~MRTlClES\n\nIsotope Detector for Steel Bearing Wear Particles\n\nIsotope wear monitors are thus a quantitative, noncontacting means of\nmonitoring the wear of rocket engine turbopump bearings and rotary seals.\nSemiconductor detectors are small (less than I cm 3 ) and lightweight (on the\norder of a few grams). Several semiconductor detectors are available which\nare 100% efficient in converting low energy y-photons into electrical signals,\nso that parts can be tagged with small, safe concentrations of low y-energy\nradioisotopes which are certified suitable for public handling by OSHA.\nDevelopment Requirements. In order to incorporate such a detector into a\nrocket engine turbopump, methods of mounting the semiconductor device and\ncapturing the wear particles Should be evaluated and tested which minimize\nthe separation of wear particles from the detector, and thus attenuation of\nthe y-photons. The methods of capture should be further evaluated for their\nefficiency in trapping wear particles. The signal to noise ratio should,be\noptimized by a proper choice of commercially available semiconductor detectors and proper design of the electronic circuitry.\n4.\n\nTunable Diode-Laser Spectrometer\n\nTechnical Description. A tunable diode-laser spectrometer is a highly compact and rugged means of measuring the infrared spectra of the constituents\nof rocket engine combustion gases. A p-n junction diode laser generates an\ninfrared beam whose wavelength is varied by altering the diode-laser bias\ncurrent. When transmitted through combustion gases, this beam is selectively\nabsorbed at certain wavelengths which are characteristic of the constituent\ngas species (Fig. 21).\n64\n\ne--.\n4.2\n\nELECTRICAL\nSIGNAL OF\nC\xc2\xb7F (TEFLON)\n\n~ ELECTRICAL\n\nI\nFigure 21.\n\nIR DETECTORS\n\nSIGNAL OF\nCO (GRAIIHITE)\n2\n\nTunable Diode Laser Spectrometer\n\nTo measure the concentration of a gas species, an optical filter (instead of\na bulky monochromator grating), consistent with an IR absorption line of the\ngas, is placed in front of a wide-band photoelectric detector. The electrical current of the detector is related to the intensity of the absorption\nline, and thus to the concentration of the gas species. Thus, for example,\nby selecting the absorption lines of HZ and O?, the combustion mixture ratio\nof HZ-fueled engines can be monitored and conErolled.\nAlternatively, the spectrometer can be used for condition monitoring. For\nexample, by selecting an absorption line of the C-F bond found in combusted\nshavings of Teflon valve seats and measuring the photocurrent in that band,\nthe concentration of the Teflon shavings or wear particles can be measured.\nSimilarly, an absorption band of CO can be selectively monitored, allowing\nZ\nmeasurement of the concentration of the debris particles from worn graphite\nrotary seals.\nOne of the principle advantages of the tunable diode spectrometer is its\nsize: the diode-laser itself can fit on top of a dime. Furthermore, while\npower of only about a milliwatt is required, the intensity of the laser output\nsignal is quite high since it is monochromatic, allowing a high inherent\nsignal-to-noise ratio. The diode-laser also has all the advantages characteristic of sem~conductor devices, inlcuding ruggedness and immunity to creep\nand fatigue.\n\n65\n\nDevelopment Requirements. To incorporate this detector into a rocket engine,\na suitable hous.ing must be designed and fabricated which isolates the detector both mechanically and thermally. Appropriate diode-laser materials and\nfilters must be chosen which correspond to the combustion constituents to be\nmonitored. The optical arrangement and electronics of the detector must be\nlaid out, fabricated and field tested. Furthermore, a lightweight shielding\nfor the detector should be designed which minimizes noise due to background\ncosmic rays. The effect, if any, of radioisotope labeling on the physical\nproperties of the parts should also be determined.\nIndirect In-Flight Sensors\n1.\n\nUltrasonic Flowmeter for Propellant Flow Measurement\n\nTechnical Description. The velocity of a sound wave is altered when it\npasses through a moving fluid, much like a boat crossing a flowing river.\nFigure 22 shows a transit-time ultrasonic flowmeter which takes advantage of\nthis principle. A pair of transducers is mounted on the pipe, directed at\none another and oriented diagonally to the flow. Ultrasonic pulses are\nalternately transmitted and detected by each of the transducers. Because the\nupstream signal velocity is decreased and the downstream velocity increased,\nthere is a difference in their velocities and thus of their transit times.\nThis difference in transit times is analyzed by electronic circuitry to\nmeasure the flow velocity. This measurement is rendered independent of\nproperties influencing sound in the fluid such as temperature, pressure,\ndensity and viscosity, by the use of a judicious combination of the measured\nupstream and downstream transit times.\n\nCOAXIAL CAlLE\n\nFLOW VELOCITY\nFLOWMETER\nMICROf\'ROCEUO\n\nc::;>\n\n\'STREAM\n\nDOWNST"~\n~\n\nVELOCITY\n\nVELOCITY\n\nBRAZED METAL WEDGE\n\nCOAXIAL CA LE\n\nFigure 22.\n\n66\n\nPIEZOELECTRIC\nlRANSMlnlR/RECEIVER\n\'AIR\n\nBlock Diagram of Transit Time Ultrasonic Flowmeter\n\nThe flowmeter has the advantage of being completely nonintrusive and thus\ncould never become an impedance to flow or a hazard in case of structural\nfailure.\nAccuracies of 0.4% and repeatabilities of 0.2% have been achieved\ncommercially.\nDevelopment Requirements. Ultrasonic flowmeters should be developed for\nsuitability in a cryogenic environment. Methods of attachment of transducers\nand piezoelectric elements must be optimized. Furthermore, since structural\nvibrations and flow noise can both produce piezoelectric signals, the frequency and acoustic polarization must be optimized to minimize these effects.\n2.\n\nUltrasonic Thermometer\n\nTechnical Description. An ultrasonic\nthermometer measures the transit time\nof an ultrasonic pulse transmitted\nacross a hot gas chamber. From this\ntransit time, the acoustic speed and,\nin turn, the gas temperature are\ndetermined. Figure 23 diagrams the\nbasic concept of this sensor. A pair\nof piezoelectric transducers alternately transmit (by means of an electronic driving signal) and receive\nultrasonic pulses sent across the gas\nchamber. The transit time of these\npulses is then determined by electronic\ncircuitry, yielding the mean acoustic\nvelocity across the chamber. From the\nacoustic velocity, the temperature of\nthe gas is determined.\nBy a judicious\ncombination of the measured transit\ntimes of pulses in both directions\nacross the chamber, the acoustic velocity determination and thus the\ntemperature measurement will be\nindependent of the gas flow speed.\n\n/ Ul TPlASONIC\n/~/\'\n\nI~IEl\n\n-_._-\n\nOEl.ECTlllC\nTil ANSDlJCEIl\n\n,/\n\n/\n\nXXXX)(-\n\n"-\n\n~UlSES\n\n1------~IElO[LfC\n\nTIlANSDlJ\n!--. -\n\nr~\n\nc~~~\n\nr-\n\nHOT GAS REGION\n\nI\n\nSIGNA~~l-\'\n\nSOUlleE\n\n[co~~~~~~~\n.--_--\'\'-~\'-.----_\n\n-1\n\n..- rllANSIT TIME\n\nSIGNAL_\n~1l0CESSIN~\n\n"-\'~E--=---=\'.:\'---SOUND S~EfD\n\n---=-c-_.\nSIGNAL\n\n~1l0CESStNG\n\n[ \xe2\x80\xa2\n\nJ\n\n{-:;:;\'M~lllAru~i.\'\\\n"-\n\nOlJT~UT , /\n\n\'---_.~\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe advantages of this sensor include\nhigh temperature capability (greater\nthan 1650 C with cooled transducers)\nand fast response. Unlike other temFigure 23. Block Diagram of\nperature sensing probes, the ultrasonic\nUltrasonic Thermometer\nthermometer measures the path-averaged\ntemperature across the gas chamber.\nIt is, furthermore, noncontacting and\nnonintrusive.\nDevelopment Outline.\nSteps for development of such a system for use in a\nrocket engine include design of electronics to optimize small-signal\nrecognition and signal-to-noise ratio, and design of a system of mounting\nand cooling the piezoelectric transducer pair.\nThe system would then be\nmounted on an engine and tested in the field.\n\n67\n\n3.\n\nDigital Quartz Pressure Sensor\n\nTechnical Description. With a digital quartz pressure sensor, one takes\nadvantage of the pressure dependence of the resonant frequency of a quartz\ncrystal. In the sensor, an electronic oscillator circuit connected to a\nquartz beam forces the beam into resonant ultrasonic oscillations. Compression of the beam alters its resonant frequency, which is then detected by the\nelectronic circuitry, generating a time-domain digital signal. Figure 24\nshows a design of a digital quartz pressure sensor for measuring absolute\npressure. The input pressure is transformed through a bellows and a cantilever into a force imposed on the quartz crystal.\nThe advantages of digital quartz pressure sensors are manifold. Quartz\ncrystal has long-term stability (no creep) ease of vibrational excitation\n(resulting in lower power consumption) and low-temperature sensitivity\n(sensitivities of 0.0057./C are experienced in quartz pressure sensors). It\nis for these very reasons that quartz oscillators are the most commonly used\nfrequency standards for clocks, etc. Additional advantages include high\nrepeatability (within 0.005i.) and a dynamic range of up to 200,000:1.\nMiniaturized digital quartz sensors are available (see Fig. 25) which can\nmeasure as high as 6.9 megapascal and weigh only 57 grams.\nDevelopment Outline. The candidate miniaturized digital quartz pressure\nsensor must be tested for compatability to engine environments. Further\ntests should be made to determine its performance trends and durability.\nThe device then can be adapted for incorporation in an engine.\n4.\n\nOptical Tachometer\n\nTechnical Description. An optical tachometer measures the rpm of a rotating\nshaft by measuring the time between reflections generated by an encoder which\nis attached to and rotates with the shaft. The configuration of an optical\ntachometer is shown in Fig. 26.\nAn LED acts as a light source, transmitting through optical fibers onto the\nencoder. The encoder, in turn, reflects the light back through a set of\noptical fibers coaxial to the input fibers, onto a semiconductor element\nwhich coqverts these light pulses into an electrical signal. To this end,\nthe encoder is appropriately bevelled and contoured to provide maximum\ncontrast between the reflective and nonreflective areas. From the known\ngeometry of the encoder and the measured time between the pulses, electronic\ncircuitry connected to the sensor determines the rpm of the shaft. The\noptical fibers are terminated "in a fused quartz rod whose end is fashioned\ninto a lens which focuses both the incident and reflected light, thus allows\nthe sensor to be placed a considerable distance from the encoder; i.e.,\noutside the liquid flow and flush with the housing wall.\nContrast ratios well above 10 and input signal to noise ratios of about 38\nhave been calculated for optical tachometers of the above description.\nBeing flush with the shaft housing, the detector is completely nonintrusive\nto cryogen in flow.\n\n68\n\n-\n\n.-...".--BAlANCE WEIGHT\nPIVOT\nQUARTZ CRYSTAL\nBEllOWS\n\nPRESSURE\n\nINPUT~\nFigure 24.\n\nDigital Quartz Pressure Sensor\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2.,\n\nFigure 25.\n\nHousing for Miniaturized Digital Quartz Pressure Sensor\n\n69\n\n;\'\n\n\' \\ :\':\' IlOTO"\n\n0.3" OIA\n\nTO VOLTAGE\nFOLLOWER\nELECTAONICS\n\n...... TTED AND\nCHA"\'FERED\nSURFACES\n\nSILICON\nDETECTOf\'l\n\nFigure 26.\n\nBlock Diagram of Optical Tachometer\n\nDevelopment Outline.\nBefore incorporation in a rocket engine turbopump, the\nlens, reflecting surfaces and fiber optics should be tested to determine\ntheir properties in the cryogenic and vibrational environment of the pump.\nDesigns for sealing the optical fibers to the fused quartz lens and for\nsealing the lens to the metal sensor port need to be evaluated.\nFinally,\ntests must be made to determine the effect of bubbles and fluid turbulence\nwhich determine the ultimate performance of this optical sensor.\nBetween Flight Inspection\nSeven technologies were chosen from the inspection techniques for the detection of prediction of the failures identified by Task I; two are considered\nrocket engine state-of-the-art, one state-of-the-art, and four are novel.\nThe technologies are holographic leak detection, thermal conductivity leak\ndetection, scanning optical pyrometry, exo-electron emission, connector\ncontinuity checking, isotope tracer detection, and particle analysis.\n\n70\n\nA brief descritpion of each of these technologies is made here.\n1.\n\nHolographic Leak Detection (Figure 27)\n\nHolographic leak detection is a technique capable of simultaneously locating\nand quantifying multiple leaks. This is achieved by illuminating the test\narea with a triple-pulse coherent light source. The first pulse reflects\noff the test object and is combined with a reference beam, obtained with\noptical beam splitters, thus forming a hologram of the object. The second\noptical pulse, shortly following the first one, forms another hologram in\nthe same manner. These holograms, recorded on the same photographic film,\ninteract to cancel each other if the object has not been altered at all.\nHowever, if the object has undergone even minute changes between the pulses,\nan interference pattern is formed. If the object is purged with a gas that\nis optically different from the ambient gas, any leak will produce such an\ninterference pattern. The volume of the interference pattern is proportional\nto the volume of the leaking gas. The third pulse of the coherent source\nenhances the interference fringes, the spacing of which are proportional to\nthe speed of the leakage. Since the product of volume and velocity is proportional to flowrate, the leakage rate can be determined simultaneously for\nall leaks in the line of sight of the detector.\nHolographic leak detection can provide significant reductions in the time\nrequired for leak detection with the advantage of being a noncontacting technique capable of locating multiple leaks simultaneously. Both qualitative\nand quantitative data reproduced in a manner which can be readily interfaced with computer processing. As an optical technique, it has the virtue\nof providing simplified scanning ability and permanent image recording.\n\nFigure 27.\n\nHolographic Leak Detection Schematic\n\n71\n\nDevelopment Outline. The development required of holographic leak detection\nbef9re its routine use on rocket engines includes establishing laser requirements and recording needs so that equipment may be selected. Real time\nimaging of the leakage hologram should also be investigated. Procedures to\ninspect the maximum number of ducts with a minimum of different viewing\nangles should be defined.\n\nTASK/YEAR\n\n1\n\n2\n\n3\n\nFEASIBILITY\nDESIGN AND FABRICATiON\nPROTOTYPE EVALUATION\n\n2.\n\nThermal Conductivity Leak Detection (Figure 28)\n\nThermal conductivity leak detection uses a hot wire resistance bridge with\none element of the bridge exposed to air\' (reference side) and the other\nelement exposed to the leak (tracer gas). Conductivity of the air changes\nwith concentration of the tracer gas which causes a change in resistance or\ntemperature of the element. Leakages are detected with a probe. Helium gas\nmay be used for the tracer. The instrument is sensitive to approximately\n1 x 10- 4 sec/sec, portable, relatively insensitive to background interference and has a good response (1 to 5 seconds). After saturation, the\ninstrument returns to zero immediately when the tracer gas source is removed.\nThis type of device, the\xc2\xb7Uson 500, has been successfully used on the J-2\nengine.\nThermal conductivity leak detection provides a fast and reliable location\nof leakage at low cost. The device is easily used and interpreted by maintenance personnel. The technique is versatile and may be used to pinpoint\nleaks indicated by other means.\nDevelopment Outline. Thermal leak detection has previously seen limited\napplication on the J-2 engine. Although these devices are commercially\navailable, an investigation to compare tracer gases and explore means of\nreducing sensitivity to background effects would improve the usability of\nthis technique on rocket engines.\n\n72\n\n....\n\nHAND-HELD\nDECTECTOR\nFLEXIBLE\nPROBE\n\nAMBIENT\nGAS\n\nTRACER\nGAS\n\nFigure 28.\n\nThermal Conductivity Detection of\nInternal Leakage\n\n1\n\nTASKIYEAR\nFEASIBILITY\n\n2\n\n3\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDESIGN AND FABRICATION\nPROTOTYPE EVALUATION\n\n.\n\n73\n\n3.\n\nScanning Optical Pyrometry (Figure 29)\n\nScanning optical pyrometry locates restrictions in chamber coolant passages\nby automatically sweeping the inner chamber walls and noting temperature\nprofile anomalies. The optical pyrometer measures the frequencies and\namplitudes of the spectrum of the thermal radiation, characteristic of the\nemissivity and temperature, emitted by the chamber wall. With blockage of a\nhot purge gas, localized cooling occurs downstream which can be detected by\nthe pyrometer. The pyrometer incorporates optical filters to isolate the\nthermal radiation into selected narrow frequency bands. This radiation is\nthen converted into electrical current by a semiconductor device. The\ncurrent is measured by an electronic system and converted to a temperature\nindication. The pyrometer automatically scans the chamber wall by a\ncombination of mechanical and optical means in both the azimuth and chamber\naxis directions. Data can be presented with imaging of the temperature\nprofile and/or by having the coordinates of all anomalies automatically\ncalculated and listed.\nScanning optical pyrometry provides fast, positive location of coolant passage restrictions. Automatic and remote operation can also provide enhanced\nsafety to maintenance personnel and, because of the noncontacting nature of\nthe scanning, less engine hazard. The technique can be used during purging\noperations to provide little impact on turnaround; its use might even permit\nreduced purging requirements.\n\nTHRUST CHAMBER\n\nAZIMUTH DRIVE\n\n__-----:::::::::il!:\'-------~\xc2\xa3.---\'\'___L,\n\nHOT\nPURGE\n\nAXIAL DRIVE\n\nFOCUS DRIVE\n\nGAS\xc2\xb7\n\nPYROMETER\nFigure 29.\n\n74\n\nScanning Pyrometer Concept for Detection of Coolant\nPassage Restrictions\n\nDevelopment Outline. In order to develop this technology for use on rocket\nengines, the measurement requirements for the pyrometer must be identified\nin terms of resolution, accuracy and range of frequency sensitivity so that\na pyrometer may be selected. A suitable scanning mechanism must be worked\nout and its control circuitry defined. The manner of data display must also\nbe selected and the appropriate electronics and software laid out.\n\nTASK/YEAR\n\n1\n\n2\n\n3\n\nFEASIBILITY\nDESIGN AND FABRICATION\nPROTOTYPE EVALUATION\n\n4.\n\n~\n\nExo-Electron Emission (Figure 30)\n\nExo-electrort emission is a means of locating and characterizing fatigue\ndamage in order to provide remaining-life prediction. This technique refers\nto the photoelectron emission from a metal part which emanates through microcracks in the brittle surface oxide. By scanning the test surface with a\nsmall beam of ultraviolet radiation, this exo-electron emission may be\ncollected and amplified by an electron multiplier and measured by a current\nmeter. The intensity of the emission increases as fatigue-induced cracks\ngrow with continued cycling. By this means, a clear, accurate indication\nof the location and progression of fatigue can be obtained.\nFatigue damage produces highly localized exo-electron emission very early,\ni.e., on the order of 17. of the fatigue life. Cracks, on the other hand,\nare not visible until about 10 to 15% of the fatigue life has expired. This\nvery early detection, as well as the characteristic increasing emission as\nfatigue damage progresses, permits correspondingly earlier and more accurate\nprediction of remaining life with the use of this technique.\nDevelopment Outline. Before exo-electron emission may be used routinely for\nrocket engine inspections, equipment suitable for in-field use may be developed. Scanning methods and sensors need to be optimized for each application.\nThe emission behavior of the materials to be inspected also must be carefully\ncharacterized. Finally, the best method of data presentation, such as plots\nor imaging, needs to be identified.\n\n75\n\n---\n\nUV L I m n - \xc2\xa3 ] SOURCE\n\n.\n\nSIGNAL\nPROCESSING\n\n-\n\ndSCANNINI1 OPTICS\nCOMPOIIENT\n\n-\n\n+I-"/\xe2\x80\xa2\nHIGH\nVOLTAGE\n\nFigure 30.\n\nSchematic of Typical Exo-Electron Inspection\n\nTASK!YEAR\nFEASIBILITY\nDESIGN AND FABRICATION\nPROTOTYPE EVALUATION\n\n76\n\n1\n\n2\n\n3\n\ns.\n\nConnector\n\nContinu~\n\nChecking\n\nConnector continuity checking verifies proper functioning of connectors by\nsimply determining the electrical continuity through them. This is done as\npart of the automatic checks that also confirm the functioning of valves,\nigniters, instrumentation, etc. The on-board controller executes a checkout\nprogram that sends signals to components (through connectors) and compares\nthe output signal to stored limit valves. Any discrepancies are noted so\nthat maintenance personnel may isolate and correct the faulty wiring, connector, or component. Connector continuity checking thus provides a fast,\nautomatic determination of connector function.\nDevelopment Outline. Connector continuity checking is presently used on the\nSpace Shuttle Main Engine. Because it performs satisfactorily, no special\ndevelopment needs are considered necessary.\n6.\n\nIsotope Tracer Detection (Figure 31)\n\nIsotope tracer detection measureS the low-energy (OSHA approved) gamma rays\nof wear particles. These particles are captured by a catcher downstream of\nthe isotope-tagged part during flight. The gamma-photons emitted by the\ncaptured particles are detected between-flight by removing the catcher from\nthe engine and placing it in a gamma-ray detector. The magnitude of the\nelectric current from the detector indicates the rate of gamma-photon\nemission and thus the quantity of wear particles.\nFLOW OF ISOTOPE\xc2\xb7TAGGED\nBEARING\xc2\xb7WEAR PARTICLES -:oillli;;;:=----~_\n\nI\n\nFLOW\n\n(=]\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n.. :\n: :\n\nMETAL SCREEN\n\n::. :\'yv\n.\n:00\n\n~/ I TRAPPED WEAR\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPAR TlCLES\n\nFigure 31.\n\nMagnetic Wear Particle Trap\n\n77\n\nTagging is accomplished by immersing the part in a flux of neutrons (e.g.,\nin a storage hold of a nuclear reactor), converting a small, uniformly distributed fraction of its atoms into radioisotopes. The part is then incubated\nfor several months, leaving it tagged with only long-lived radioisotopes\nsuitable for long term monitoring of wear.\nMaterials chosen for bearings are often ferromagnetic because of desirable\nmechanical properties, so debris from parts made of such material could be\ncaptured by a magnetic trap. An alternate method would route the purging\nor cooling fluid through a duct to a lower-pressure region where a lowpressure-drop filter catches a representative amount of the wear particles.\nIsotope tracer detection thus offers a quantitative, noncontacting means of\nmonitoring the wear of components. Isotope tagging provides excellent identification of the wear particles of a selected component in an environment\nwhere wear particles generated from several different parts might be present.\nDevelopment Outline. In order to apply isotope tracer detection to rocket\nengine components, methods of capturing the wear particles must be evaluated\nand tested for efficiency lin entrapment and retention. The effect, if any,\nof radioisotope tagging on the physical properties of the components should\nbe determined. Engine performance effects must be evaluated. Selection of\na rapid and reliable ground-based gamma-ray measurement system should also\nbe investigated.\n\nTASK/YEAR\n\n1\n\n2\n\n3\n\nFEASIBILITY\nDESIGN AND FABRICATION\nPROTOTYPE EVALUATION\n\n7.\n\nParticle Analysis (Figure 32)\n\nParticle analysis is a means of determining wear or contamination by examining the debris in a working fluid. These particles are captured in-flight\nand analyzed between-flight. The analysis identifies the concentration of\neach type of particle material so that, with the materials of engine components known, the upstream wear or contaminant source can be monitored.\nSelection of different materials for wear surfaces of different parts\nprovides good wear isolation. By trending the wear history of each\ncomponent, sudden excessive wear or incipient failure can be identified\nand appropriate actions taken.\n\n78\n\n..\n\n.1\n\nI\nI\nPARTICLE SIZE\n\n,\n\nI,\';"\n\n",t--- -- - - - - - - -- - - - - - - - - - - --1\n/ I\nPARTICLE UANTITY\n\nw\n\nLU\n\n:::>\n\'"\n~\n\nrt\n\n-J\n\n-.:\n\nE\nz\n\n.....\nREAK IN\n\n13\n:;;:\nILL\nu\n::c\n\n~\n\n113\n\n\'"\nIV>\n;=\nl-\n\nl~\n\nI\n\nNORMAL OPE\nOPERATING TIME\n\nFigure 32.\n\nTypical Wear Particle Trending\n\nMaterials chosen for bearings are often ferromagnetic because of desirable\nmechanical properties, so debris from parts made of such material could be\ncaptured by a magnetic trap. An alternate method would route the purging or\ncooling fluid through a duct to a lower-pressure region where a low-pres suredrop filter catches a representative amount of the wear particles.\nThe analysis of the particles can be accomplished in several ways, the most\ncommon being spectrometry. The particles are identified as to their material makeup and the corresponding concentrations of each material. Trending\nof wear or identification of contaminants can then be used to make timely\nmaintenance actions.\nParticle analysis thus offers a quantitative noncontacting means of monitoring wear or contamination. Trending of wear data can permit use of components for their full life as well as enhanced maintenance planning capability.\n\n79\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDevelopment Outline. Before particle analysis can be applied to rocket\nengines, methods of capturing wear particles and contaimnants must be\nevaluated and tested for efficiency in entrapment and retention. This\nwould include an investigation of performance effects on the engine.\nSelection of a rapid and reliable ground-based particle analyzer should\nalso be evaluated.\n\n2\n\nTASK!YEAR\n\n3\n\nFEASIBILITY\nDESIGN AND FABRICATION\n\n_\n\nPROTOTYPE EVALUATION\n\nAI\n\n80\n\nSELECTION OF PREFERRED IN-FLIGHT OR BETWEEN-FLIGHT TECHNOLOGY\nThe highest ranked technology selections that were considered upgradable for\nboth in-flight and between-flight, and the effort required to develop these\ntechnologies have been identified. A comparison is now made between these\ntechnologies for a given failure mode and the preferred technology selected.\nIn two cases, however, technologies have been combined to form:\n\n.\n\nWear Detection\n\n2.\n\n...\n\n1.\n\nIn-Flight Indirect Condition Monitoring\n\nWEAR DETECTION\nThe two technologies used to detect wear are:\n1.\n\nIsotope wear detectors\n\n2.\n\nTunable diode-laser spectrometer\n\nWear can be monitored by tagging certain engine parts, i.e., turbopump\nbearings. The debris from these parts can be captured in-flight and recorded\nor the debris may be entrapped and retained and measured between flights.\nDetails of the capturing system remain to be defined.\nThe tunable diode-laser spectrometer can be used for detecting wear for\ncondition monitoring by selecting absorption lines for appropriate materials\nwhich would be indicative of wear, i.e., the C-F bond corresponding to Teflon\nvalve seats.\nThe combination of these two technologies will provide data on surface wear\nand to identify the wear surface within the engine. The detection can either\nbe in-flight and/or between-flight. In any event, detectable wear would be\ncause for further investigation between-flight.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe need exists to identify liquid rocket engine internal wear surfaces and\nwear rates. One of the differences between air breathing engines and liquid\nrocket engines is that the latter currently have no procedure to indicate\nsurface wear, as contrasted to SOAP (Spectrographic Oil Analysis Procedure)\nfor air breathing engine lubricating systems. The need exists to identify\nliquid rocket engine internal wear surfaces and wear rates. The combination\nof these two technologies provides such a possibility. The system would\nsatisfy an existing need, be fail-safe, non-intrusive, with medium-to-longdevelopment time and effort. It is strongly recommended.\nIN-FLIGHT INDIRECT CONDITION MONITORING\n\nAn in-flight indirect condition monitoring system acquires performance data\non the engine fluid path and rotating mechanical parameters. This in-flight\ndata can be used to predict, detect, and diagnose failures after it has been\nvalidated, corrected, displayed and interpreted. The objective is to predict,\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n81\n\ndetect and diagnose prior to loss in mission capability. The important result\nfrom an engine monitoring system is \'the effect of the information on the maintenance system. Air breathing engine monitoring systems have been designed\nwith various degrees of sophisticated electronic systems, automatic recording,\namount of data, etc., and with various degrees of success.\nThe basic required data of the fluid path is the f10wrates, pressures, and\ntemperatures at each desired station. The data for the rotating mechanical\nequipment is the rpm.\nFrom Task II, four technologies were recommended for in-flight indirect\ncondition monitoring:\n1.\n\nUltrasonic flow meter\n\n2.\n\nUltrasonic thermometer\n\n3.\n\nDigital quartz pressure sensor\n\n4.\n\nOptical tachometer\n\nAn assessment has been made that the current pressure sensors, even though\nthey may have some less than desirable characteristics, are adequate to meet\nthe engine condition monitoring requirements; and similarly, adequate tachometers also exist. Therefore, technologies No. 3 and 4, digital quartz pressure sensor and optical tachometer, will not be considered further.\nWith these two combined technologies, each failure mode will be discussed and\nthe selected technology noted, as well as the reasons for the selection.\nFailure Mode 2:\n\nCoolant Passage Leakage/Restriction\n\nThe in-flight sensor depends upon the measurement of several f10wrates and\nfinding a small difference of two large numbers, while the between-flight\ntechnology consists of locating coolant passage restrictions prior to the\ngeneration of a hot spot and the probable burn-through of the cooling passage.\nThe primary problem of locating a coolant passage restriction is the fact that\nthe restriction must be located by viewing from the outside of the passage.\nThis problem has always existed and, in general, is similar to the problem of\nrestrictions occurring in air breathing engine turbine air cooled blades. The\nproposed solution approach is similar - locating an internal passage restriction by the measurement of the external surface temperature. Under some conditions, this solution approach has been successful for air breathing engine\nturbine air cooled blades.\nThe selection is the Scanning Optical Pyrometer of between-flight technology.\nFailure Mode 3:\n\nJoint Leakage\n\nSince this failure mode does not lend itself to an in-flight sensor, the\nholographic leak detection was selected.\n\n82\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFailure Mode 5:\n\nHigh Torque Turbopump\n\nBoth in-flight and between-flight suggest a wear detector. A combination of\nthis technology, and with existing rpm tail-off and break-away torque would\nimprove detection of a high torque.\nFailure Mode 6:\n\nCracked Turbine Blades\n\nThe selection was the optical pyrometer of the in-flight sensors and was the\nchoice of a state-of-the-art technology over a novel technology. Currently\nthe optical pyrometer is being used in several air breathing turbo-fan engines\nwith a large degree of success. The pyrometer can detect, follow, and control\nturbine blade material temperature transients, as well as detect certain failure modes for cooled turbine blades. It is proving a very valuable sensor.\nRocket engines historically experience very large temperature transients\n(often thousands of degrees per second). This is associated with the rapid\nstart and cut-off transient brought about by the application requirements and\nthe engine control system. These large temperature transients result in large\nthermal stresses. They have existed in the past, they exist today, and probably will exist in the near future. The result is failed turbine blades. It\nis anticipated in the near future that cooled turbine blades will be incorporated into liquid rocket engines.\nTherefore, the optical pyrometer is needed currently and within the foreseeable\nfuture. The optical pyrometers can be incorporated in liquid rocket engines\nwith a large pay-off, non-intrusive, fail-safe, short development time, minimum\neffort, and is state of the art. It is very strongly recommended.\nFailure Mode 7:\n\nCracked Convolution Bellows\n\nThe in-flight sensor, a digital quartz pressure sensor, depends upon measuring\nthe pressure difference between a cracked and an uncracked convolution. The\nbetween-flight technique would be exo-electron emission as a means of locating\nand characterizing fatigue damage. The current between-flight technique would\nbe a baroscope inspection. The between-flight inspection technique is preferred and the existing procedure appears adequate.\nFailure Mode 8:\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nLoose Electrical Connector\n\nNo reasonable in-flight sensor or technique was determined. The procedure\nwould then be performed between-flight and the recommended technique would be\nconnector continuity checking.\nFailure Mode 9:\n\nBearing Damage\n\nDamage to the bearing probably occurs under dynamic conditions and could be\ndetected by the in-flight fiberoptic deflectometer permitting corrective action\neither in-flight or between-flight. The wear detector (isotope tracer detection) inspected between-flight would also indicate bearing damage. However,\nbetween-flight inspection does not permit the option of corrective action inflight. Therefore, the in-flight sensor is preferred.\n\n83\n\nFailure Mode 10:\n\nTube Fracture\n\nNo reasonable in-flight sensor or technique was determined. The between-flight\ntechnique was exo-e1ectron emission. The current between-flight technique\nwould be an optical inspection for fluid leaks and is the preferred betweenflight inspection technique.\nFailure Mode 11:\n\nTurbopump Seal Leakage\n\nBoth the in-flight and between-flight technologies are wear detection; however.\nthe existing technology consists of pressurizing the seal cavity with an inert\ngas between flights and measuring the pressure decay. which can be related\ndirectly to a leakage rate. Historically. acceptable leakage rates have been\nestablished and if the leakage rate exceeds acceptable limits. corrective\naction is indicated. Corrective action in-flight based upon wear detection\nand wear rates is very remote. The existing technology will be retained and\nwear detection will be added for between-flight inspection.\nFailure Mode 13:\n\n..\n\nValve Fails to Perform\n\nThe in-flight selection is for the "Indirect Condition Honitoring". The\nperformance data along the fluid path would indicate the failure of the valve\nto perform and permit early corrective action. The between-flight inspection\ntechnology would not permit this possibility of early corrective action.\nTherefore, the in-flight technique is preferred.\nFailure Mode 14:\n\nInternal Valve Leakage\n\nThe in-flight selection is for indirect condition monitoring. The performance\ndata on the fluid path would indicate the internal valve leakage failure and\npermit early corrective action. The between-flight inspection technology\nwould not permit this possibility of early corrective action. Therefore. the\nin-flight technique is preferred.\nI\n\nFailure Mode 15:\n\nRegulator Discrepancies\n\nThe in-flight selection is for indirect condition monitoring. The performance\ndata along the fluid path would indicate a regulator discrepancy and permit\nearly corrective action. The between-flight inspection technology would not\npermit this possibility of early corrective action. Therefore. the in-flight\ntechnique is preferred.\nFailure Mode 16:\n\nContaminated Hydraulic Control Assembly\n\nSince this failure mode does not lend itself to an in-flight sensor. the\nparticle analysis of between-flight inspection technology was selected. These\nselections are shown in Table 20.\n\n84\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nTABLE\nFAILURE\nMODE\nCATEGORY\n\n20.\n\nUPGRADABLE TECHNOLOGY SELECTION\nUPGRADABLE TECHNOLOGY\n\nFAILURE MODE\nOESCRI PTION\n\nIN-FLIGHT (I)\n\nBETWEEN- FLI GHT (B)\n\nSELECTED\n\nULTRASONIC FLOWMETER\n\nSCANNING OPTICAL\nPYROMETER\n\nB\n\n-\n\nHOLOGRAPHIC LEAK\nDETECTOR\n\nB\n\n2\n\n3\n\nJOINT LEAKAGE\n\n5\n\nHIGH TORQUE TURBOPUMP\n\n6\n\nCRACKED TURBINE BLADES PYROMETER\n\n7\n\nCRACKED CONVOLUTIONS\nBELLOWS\n\nB\n\nLOOSE ELECTRICAL\nCONNECTOR\n\n9\n\nBEARING DAMAGE\n\n10\n\nTUBE FRACTURE\n\n11\n\nTURBOPUMP SEAL LEAKAGE WEAR DETECTOR (TUNABLE\nDIODE SPECTOMETER)\n\nPRESSURE DELAY (EXISTING)\nWEAR DETECTOR (ISOTOPE\nTRACER DETECTION)\n\n13\n\nVALVE FAILS TO PERFORM\n\nWEAR DETECTOR (ISOTOPE\nTRACER DETECTION)\n\nI\n\n14\n\n...\n\nCOOLANT PASSAGE\nLEAKAGE RESTRICTION\n\nINTERNAL VALVE LEAKAGE\n\nTHERMAL CONDUCTIVITY LEAK\nDETECTION\n\nI\n\nTHERMAL CONDUCTIVITY LEAK\nDETECTION\n\nI\n\nDIGITAL QUARTZ PRESSURE\nSENSOR\n\n-\n\n-\n\n/\n\nINDIRECT\n\n< CONDITION\n\nMONITORING\nREGULATOR DISCREPANCIES\n\n16\n\nCONTAMINATED HYDRAULIC\nCONTROL ASSEMBLY\n\nBOTH\n\nI\n\nBOROSCOPE (EXISTING)\nEXO-ELECTRON EMISSION\n\nB\n(EXIST! Failure Mode No.3, Joint Leakage,\naccounts for almost half the failures (45%). The other three large\nfrequencies are Failure Mode No.2, Coolant Passage Leakage/Restriction (15%), Failure Mode No. 14, Internal Valve Leakage (12%), and\nFailure Mode No. 11, Turbopump Seal Leakage (8.5%). The first two\nFailure Modes (3 and 2) result in 60% of the total failures and the\nfirst four Failure Modes in over 80% of the total failures. Three\nof these four are between-flight technologies. Clearly, these\nupgradable technologies are very important.\nThe second selection is based upon the magnitude of development\neffort required. Assuming limited developmental effort, which\ntechnology can be established for the least effort? The first is\nthe pyrometer for detecting turbine blade temperatures in-flight.\nThe second is the fiberoptic deflectometer for measuring turbopump\nbearing loads and damage in flight. The third is the indirect\ncondition monitoring system. The first three are all in-flight\ntechnologies. The last three are all between-flight technologies\nand are wear detector, scanning optical pyrometer, and holographic\nleak detector.\n\nTABLE\n\n21.\n\nPRIORITY SELECTION LIST FOR UPGRADABLE this report.\n1.\n\nApproximately 85.000 liquid rocket engine failure reports. from\n30 years of developing and delivering major pump fed engines. were\nreviewed and screened. and reduced to 1771. These were categorized\ninto 16 different failure modes.\nThese failure modes were common\nto all engines and historically consistent.\n\n2.\n\nThe state of the art of engine condition monitoring for in-flight\nsensors and between-flight inspection technology was determined.\n\n3.\n\nFailure propagation diagrams for the 16 failure modes were established; the potential measurands and diagnostic requirements were\nidentified and compiled. The sensors and inspection technology\nwere matched with the measurands and requirements.\n\n4.\n\nThe sensor and inspection technology was assessed and ranked.\n\n5.\n\nAreas requiring advanced technology development have been identified and are as follows:\na.\n\nDirect In-Flight Condition Monitoring\n(1)\n\nOptical Pyrometer - turbine blade temperatures\nVery strongly recommended\n\n(2)\n\nFiberoptic Deflectometer - bearing condition\nVery strongly recommended\n\n(3)\n\nIsotope Wear Detector - wear particles\nStrongly recommended\n\n(4)\n\nTunable Diode Laser Spectrometer - wear particles\nStrongly recommended\n\nb.\n\nIndirect In-Flight Condition Monitoring\n(5)\n\nUltrasonic Flowmeter - propellant flow\nVery strongly recommended\n\n(6)\n\nUltrasonic Thermometer - high temperatures\nVery strongly recommended\n\nc.\n\nBetween-Flight Inspection Techniques\n(7)\n\nHolographic Leak Detector - fluid leaks\nStrongly recommended\n\n(8)\n\nScanning Pyrometer - blocked fluid passages\nRecommended\n\n88\n\nAPPENDIX A. GENERAL DESCRIPTION OF\nCONVENTIONAL ROCKET ENGINES\n\nAPPENDIX A\nGENERAL DESCRIPTION OF CONVENTIONAL ROCKET ENGINES\nConventional rocket engines are defined as bipropellant engines that use liquid\noxygen and a hydrocarbon based fuel (RP-l) for combustion (Fig. 33). The propellants are fed into the thrust chamber (the major engine component that transforms, through combustion, the potential energy stored in the fuel into kinetic\nenergy by means of centrifugal pumps powered by gas generator-driven turbines.\nThe flow of propellants is regulated by valves which, in turn, are controlled by\nhydraulic, electrical and/or pneumatic systems to establish required valve actuation relationship for engine operation. An ignition system, either by means of\nhypergolic pyrotechnics or electrical spark plugs, initiates combustion in the\nthrust chamber and gas generator. Most engine systems are provided ~ith a purge\nsystem that protects some components from contamination by the external environment or protect critical areas against mixture of potential hazardous leakage of\npropellants. Other subsystems might be found on conventional engines depending\non their cycles of operation or state-of-the-art development.\nThe conventional rocket engines (Fig. 34 ) referred to in this study are: Atlas\nMA-3 and MA-5, Thor, RS-27, F-l and H-I. The J-2 engine is a more advanced type\nof propulsion system since it uses hydrogen as fuel, yielding a higher specific\nimpulse (426 sec/vacuum). The SSME propulsion system, again using hydrogen as\nfuel, is another step forward in state of the art, and uses a two-stage combustion\nprocess requiring higher pressures and making a more efficient use of propellants.\nA brief description and application of these propulsion systems follows.\nSPACE SHUTTLE MAIN ENGINE (SSME)\nThe high performance requirements of the SSME engine demand the use of a staged\ncombustion power cycle (Fig. 35) coupled with high combustion chamber pressures.\nIn the SSME staged combustion power cycle, the propellants are partially burned\nat low mixture ratio, very high pressure, and relatively low temperatures in the\npreburners to produce hydrogen-rich gas to power the turbopumps. The hydrogenrich steam is then routed to the main injector where it is injected, along with\nadditional oxidizer and fuel, into the main combustion chamber at a high mixture\nratio and high pressure. Hydrogen fuel is used to cool all combustion devices\ndirectly exposed ~o contact with high-temperature combustion products. An electronic engine controller automatically performs checkout, start, mainstage and\nengine shutdown functions.\nThe SSME was developed especially for the Space Shuttle Orbiter vehicle, which\nuses three systems for launch. The SSME is a reusable, high performance, liquid\npropellant rocket engine with variable thrust. The engine is ignited on the\nground at launch and operates in parallel with the solid rocket boosters during\nthe initial ascent phase and continues to operate for approximately 480 seconds\ntotal firing duration. Each of the rocket engines operates on a mixture ratio\nof 6:1, and a chamber pressure of 3000 psia to produce a sea-level thrust of 375K\npounds of thrust. The engine is throttleable over a thrust range of 65 to 109i.\nof design thrust level. This provides a higher thrust level during liftoff and\nthe initial ascent phase, and allows orbiter acceleration to be limited to 3 g\nduring the final ascent phase. The engines are gimbaled to provide pitch, yaw,\nand roll control during orbiter boost phase.\n89\n\n\\()\n\no\n\nON.()FF\nISOLATION VALVE\nORIFICE\n\nFUEL\n\nFUEL TANK\n\n,UMP\n\nON.()FF VALVE\n\n/--.\n\n/\n\\\n\nr\n\nORIFICE\n\nGAS\nGENERATOR\nORIFICE\n\n1I\nOXIDIZER\n\nOXIDIZER\nTANK\n\n\'UW\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nON.()FF VALVE\n\nORIFICE\nON.()FF\nISOLATION VALVE\n\nFigure\n\n33.\n\nSimplified Existing Control System, Gas Generator Engine\n\n\\D\nf-\'\n\nFigure\n\n34.\n\nConventional Rocket Engines\n\nON.QFF ISOLATION VALVE\n\nI\n\nFUEl\nTANK\n\nL-....II\\I\'L-....;....J\n\nl.OW~REISURE\n\nFUel.\nTURIIOI"UMP\n\n...\n\nVALVE OPENING\nSCHEDULE f (f\'OWER)\n\nVALVE OPENING\nSCHEDULE f(f\'OWERI\n\nHIGH\n"ReSSURe\nFUEL\nTUftlQflUMP\n\nI.---~. - - - - 1\n\n%\\ - - - - - - - - 1 - - - - - - ,\n\nFLOWMETER\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nt\n\n1\n\n!\n\nFUEl.\n-\n\n.~IVL~ . . AND Pc)\n\n~""\'----\'\n\nFEED~CK ~l\n\nCOMPUTER\nTH"UST CONTROL VALVE\n(Pc)\n\n-+ OXIDIZER\n;:::..IM--4 PRe.URNE"\n\n1\n1---1\n\nON.QFF\nISOLATION VALVE\n\nOXIDIZER\nTANK\n\n....\n\nLOW\n\np"Essu..e\n\nOXIDize.. \'\nTU"IIOPUMP\n\nHIGH\nPRe.URe\nOXIDIZE"\nTUftM>PUMI"\n\nVALVE~NING\n\nSCHEDULE f (f\'OWE"1\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 3 SCHEDULED AND 2 CONTROLLED VALVES FOR MR\nAND THRUST LEVEL\n\nFigure 35.\n\nSimplified Engine Control System, Staged Combustion Engine\n\n\'"\n\nJ-2 ENGINE\nThe J-2 rocket engine is a high-performance, multiple-restart engine that uses\nliquid oxygen for oxidizer and liquid hydrogen for fuel. Each propellant is\npumped into the thrust chamber by separate gas-turbine-driven, direct-drive turbopumps. The two turbopumps are powered in series by a single gas generator that\nuses the same propellants as the thrust chamber. The thrust chamber is tubularwalled and is regeneratively cooled by circulating fuel through the tubes before\nthe fuel is injected into the combustion area. The engine has a refillable start\ntank from which pressurized gaseous hydrogen is routed to the turbopump turbines\nfor starting the engine. This feature, combined with the augmented spark ignition\nsystem, makes the J-2 a multistart engine.\nThe J-2 engines were ignited at altitude as they powered the second stage of the\nSaturn V Vehicle and developed 225K pounds of thrust. The burn duration was\n480 seconds with a mixture ratio of 5.50 (O/F). It yielded a specific impulse\n(I ) of 423.8.\nsp\nThe J-2 rocket engine was developed to provide the power for the SIVB stage of the\nSaturn IB vehicle and for the SII and SIVB stages of the Saturn V vehicle.\nThe SII stage is propelled by a cluster of five J-2 engines, four outboard engines\nand one inboard engine. Because only a single engine start is required for SII\nstage application, the engines are modified to delete the engine restart capability\nby blocking off the start tank refill lines. The inboard and outboard engines are\nbasically identical.\nThe SIVB stage is propelled by one J-2 engine. The engine used in the Saturn IB,\nSIVB stage, and the engine used in the Saturn V, SIVB stage are basically identical.\nH-I ENGINE\nThe H-I rocket engine is a single-start, fixed thrust, pump-fed, regeneratively\ncooled liquid bipropellant engine that uses liquid oxygen and RP-l fuel. The\npropellants are supplied to the gimbal-mounted thrust chamber by a gas generatordriven turbopump that has two centrifugal pumps on a single shaft. The engine is\ncalibrated to develop a sea-level rated thrust of 205,000 pounds of thrust with an\nI sp of 263.4 seconds at a mixture ratio of 2.23:1 oxidizer/fuel. The B-1 engine\nwas ignited on the ground and the scheduled burn duration was 150 seconds.\nThe H-l engine was developed to boost the Saturn IB vehicles. In the first stage\nbooster, eight B-1 engines were used in a two-concentric arrangement, four outboard, and four inboard. The outboard engines were capable of being gimbaled for\npitch, yaw, and roll control. At launch the vehicle was held down until satisfactory mainstage combustion was established in all eight engines.\n\n93\n\nF-l ENGINE\nThe F-l engine is a single-start, fixed thrust, liquid bipropellant engine, calibrated to develop a sea-level-rated thrust of 1,522,000 pounds with an I sp of 265\nseconds during 150 seconds of operation. Engine propellants are liquid oxygen and\npropellant RP-l (kerosene) fuel at a mixture ratio of 2.27:1. The propellant fuel\nis used as the working fluid for the gimbal actuators and for the engine control\nsystem, and is also used as the turbopump bearing lubricant.\nThe F-l engine features a two-piece thrust chamber that is tubular-walled and\nregeneratively cooled to the 10:1 expansion ration plane, and double-walled and\nturbine gas cooled to the 16:1 expansion ration plane; a thrust chamber mounted\nturbopump that has two centrifugal pumps on a single shaft driven by a two-stage,\ndirect-driven turbine; one-piece rigid propellant ducts that are used in pairs to\ndirect the fuel and oxidizer to the thrust chamber; and a hypergolic fluid cartridge that is used for thrust chamber ignition.\nThrust vector changes are achieved by gimbaling the entire engine. The gimbal\nblock is located on the thrust chamber dome, and actuator attach points are provided by two outriggers on the thrust chamber body.\nThe F-l propulsion system was developed to provide the power for the booster\nflight phase of the Saturn V vehicle. Five engines are clustered in the S-IC\nstage of the Saturn V to obtain the necessary 7,610,000 pounds thrust. The\nignition of the engine takes place on the ground and the vehicle was not released\nuntil satisfactory mainstage operation was established in all five engines.\nRS-27 ENGINE\nThe RS-27 rocket engine is, like the H-l engine, a single-start, fixed thrust,\npump-fed, regeneratively cooled liquid bipropellant engine that used liquid\noxygen and RP-l fuel. The propellants are supplied to the gimbal-mounted thrust\nchamber by a gas generator driven turbopump that has two centrifugal pumps on a\nsingle shaft. The engine is calibrated to develop a sea-level thrust of 205K\npounds of thrust with an lsp of 262.7 seconds and a mixture ratio of 2.24:1\noxidizer/fuel.\nThe RS-27 engine system was developed from H-l and Thor engines hardware, to serve\nas a booster engine for the Delta Launch vehicle.\nThe engine is ignited on the ground and for over 240 seconds operates in parallel\nwith a number of solid propellant booster rockets clustered around the vehicle.\nIts mission is to power the first stage of the Delta launch vehicle used to place\nin orbit a variety of commercial satellites.\n\n94\n\nTHOR ENGINE\nThe Thor engine is like the RS-27, a single-start fixed thrust, pump-fed, regeneratively cooled liquid engine that used liquid oxygen and RP-l fuel. The propellants are supplied to the gimbal mounted thrust chamber by a gas generator-driven\nturbopump that has two centrifugal pumps, on a single shaft. The latest version\nof the engine is calibrated to develop a sea-level thrust of l70K pounds of thrust\nwith an I\nof 247 seconds and a mixture ratio of 2.30:1, oxidizer/fuel.\nsp\nThe Thor engine, developed in the late 1950\'s, was designed to serve as the main\npropulsion system for the Thor missile.\nLately, the Thor, with a burn duration of 175 seconds, has been used to orbit\npayloads.\nATLAS ENGINE\nThe Atlas propulsion system is composed of two separate types of engines, booster\nand sustainer. The booster consists of two low altitude thrust chambers with\ntheir components, similar to the H-l or RS-27 engines. The sustainer engine is a\nhigh-performance propulsion system designed for high-altitude operation. The\ndesign of the system is such that the booster engines deliver 330K of thrust and\nburn of 145 seconds, the sustainer engines have a thrust of 57K and operate for\n320 seconds.\nThe Atlas engine, MA-3 version, was developed to power the ICBM missiles in the\nlate 1950s. An uprated version, the MA-5 engine, was developed for use as an\nintermediate launch vehicle.\nThe Atlas engine systems yield a specific impulse of 258 seconds for the booster\nand 219 for the sustainer. The mixture ratio for both enginesis 2.25.\nCurrently the Atlas MA-3 version E/F series is used to boost classified payloads.\nThe MA-5 engine systems are incorporated into NASA\'s SLV-3D launch vehicle that is\nused to place satellites in orbit .\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n95\n\nA....ENDIX B. FAILURE SUMMARY SHEETS\n\nAPPENDIX B\nFAILURE SUMMARY SHEETS\nEach failure mode that passed the UCR screening process has been documented as\none of the following sheets. Each sheet, one per each failure mode, records the\nfailure mode and cause, the frequency of failure, the design life, if available,\nand the effect of the failure upon the subsystem and/or engine. Further identification is shown with respect to the failure type, criticality, reaction time,\nand the detection method used. An evaluation is made with respect to failure\npredictability, and potential measurands of the parameters that are affected by\nthe failure.\nBased on these elements, a selection of suitable in-flight monitoring systems and between-flight inspection techniques is presented for each failure\nmode. All these data are recorded in a format that was approved and found appropriate for conducting this study.\n\n97\n\nFACTOR\nIDENTI FI CATION\n(i1)\n\nFACTORS\nCOMPONENT NM1E - EVALUATION CONDUCTED AT THE ENGINE SYSTEM AND COMPONENT OR LINE\nREPLACEMLE UNIT (LRU) LEVEL. FAILURE PROPAGATION\nBLOCK DIAGRAMS\n\nAPPENDIX C\nFAILURE PROPAGATION BLOCK DIAGRAMS\nThe Failure Propagation Block Diagrams were devised to obtain a better understanding of the failure mechanism of each of the sixteen failure modes encountered in\nthe study.\nEach diagram attempts to illustrate the events which lead to the failure as\ndescribed in the Failure Summary sheets (see Appendix B). It has been found that\nby indicating symptoms and events preceding the outright failure, the determination\nof appropriate monitoring devices is made easier. There is a Failure Propagation\nBlock diagram for each of the sixteen failure modes, regardless of engine system.\nThe events are shown as rectangles and the time sequence from left to right.\n\ne80LT TO"OOE "ELAX~TION\n\n\'\'\'EM~TU\'\'E\n\nCUT\xc2\xb7OFF\n\nIVIIIR~TION\nS~FETYI\n\n....\n\nVI."~TION\n\n~\n\n"ET~INING\nC~VIT~TION 10-\n\nSCREW\nLOOSENING\n\n4\n\n~COUSTICS\n\nI--\n\n----.\n\n--\n\nFRETTING\n\nr----.\n\nFIRE\nEXPLOSION\n\n-------\n\nMain Oxidizer Valve\n\n215\n\nFAILU..E MOOE 1\xc2\xb7\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SOLT TO..QUE ..ELAXATION\n\n....ESSU..ANT\nLEAKS INTO\nGGCV\xc2\xb7\nACTUATO"\n\nSEQUENCE\nVALVE SEAL\nRETAINE..\n\nr\n\nGGCV\xc2\xb7\nACTUATES\n....EMATURELy\n\nDETONATION\nIN GAS\nGENERATOR\n\nIGNITION_\nSOURCE\n\n\xc2\xb7GASGENE..ATO.. IN-FLIGHT CONDITION\nMONITORING LITERATURE SEARCH\n\nAPPENDIX E\nIN-FLIGHT CONDITION MONITORING LITERATURE SEARCH\nA computerized search and review of periodicals was employed to survey the\nliterature for identification of novel and state-of-the-art in-flight conditionmonitoring technologies. The in-house on-line capability included Orbit IV and\nDialog systems, and Compendex, NTIS, and ISMEC data bases, totaling some six\nmillion citations.\nThe search addressed all those citations which were related to sensors, instruments and detectors, both diagnostic and prognostic. It was limited to industrial,\naerospace and automotive fields and the result was 289 relevant citations. Upon\nreviewing these citations, 89 complete articles were requested. They are\nsummarized in Table 22 according to article title, author, source, in-flight/\nbetween-flight novel, SOTA and rocket-engine categories. The table also shows\nthe number of SOTA and novel,in-flight and between-flight condition-monitoring\nsystems discussed in each article.\nThis search combined with a few other minor on-line searches and review of periodicals resulted in the 20 novel and 14 SOTA technologies.\n\n231\n\nN\nW\n00\n\nTABLE 22.\n\nSUMMARY LITERATURE SEARCH FOR IN-FLIGHT CONDITION MONITORING TECHNOLOGIES\n----_._\'E1WEE~UOHT\n\n1M-FUGItT\n\n...\nc"\n\n.. IN\xc2\xb7FLIGHT CONDITION\nMONITORING SENSOR GRADING\n\nAPPENDIX G\nIN-FLIGHT CONDITION,MONITORING SENSOR GRADING\nIn this section, candidate sensors are graded with respect to ideal sensors for\ntheir usefulness in detecting each class of rocket engine failure. The grading\nis based on the lumped technical and economic descriptors described in Table 23,\nincluding physical, electronic and functional requirements, detectability, durability, safety and cost. The development time required for each sensor to be lIsed\nfor the given application is also listed, and an overall grade is given, again\nwith respect to an ideal sensor.\n\nTABLE 23.\n\n..\n\nLUMPED DESCRIPTORS\n\nTECHNICAL REOUIREMENTS:\n\nTECHNICAL FEATURES:\n-----..------\n\nECONOMICAL:\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 PHYSICAL\n\xe2\x80\xa2 WEIGHT\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SPACE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 STRENGTH\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MATERIALS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 CHEMICALS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 RESONANCE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 FATIGUE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 DETECTABILITY\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SPEED\n\xe2\x80\xa2 ACCURACY\n\xe2\x80\xa2 REPEATABILITY\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SENSITIVITIY\n\xe2\x80\xa2 RESOLUTION\n\xe2\x80\xa2 DRIFT\n\xe2\x80\xa2 ARTIFACTS\n\xe2\x80\xa2 SUSCEPTIBILITY\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 EXPENDITURES\n\xe2\x80\xa2 R&D\n\xe2\x80\xa2 INTEGRATION\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 ELECTRONIC\n\xe2\x80\xa2 POWER. LITERATURE SURVEYED FOR\nBETWEEN-FLIGHT INSJOECTION\n\nAPPENDIX H\nLITERATURE SURVEYED FOR BETHEEN-FLIGHT INSPECTION\nA survey was undertaken to find inspection technology which could be applicable\nto reusable rocket engines between flights. This survey included computer literature searches, periodical reviews, and personal visits. Representative literatlJre\nwas enumerated and the inspection techniques uncovered were then summarized. This\nappendix indicates the 56 documents that were tabulated as a result of this survey.\nThe sequence shown represents only the order in which the literature was reviewed.\n\n...\n\nTable 24 is a tabulation indicating the title, author(s), source organization\nwhich actually performed the study or tests, level of development (Novel, Rocket\nEngine, or state-of-the-art), and remarks for each document .\nTable 25 gives the reference information needed to obtain the literature,\nlisted to correspond to the tabulation numbering of Table 24 \xe2\x80\xa2\n\n..\n\n277\n\nN\n\'-I\n\nTABLE 24.\n\nSUMMARY OF LITERATURE SEARCH FOR BETWEEN-FLIGHT CONDITION MONITORING TECHNOLOGIES\n\n00\n\nIN\xc2\xb7FLlGHT\nI-\n\n\'"\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 I-\n\nAUTHOR\n\nTITLE\n\nNUMIER\n\nC\'"\n1-"\n\nGOE,\n\nSOURCE\n\n51i\n\n~~\nOil:\n.. z\n0\nz\n\nIETWEEN\xc2\xb7FLIGHT\n\n.\n..\n~\n0\n\nz\n\n..\n\'"\n\nC\n\nI-\n\n51\n\n>\n0\n\n1\n\nMAINTAINA~ILITY\nSHUTTLE OR8ITE~\n\n2\n\nDIVE~SIFICATION\n\nOF ACOUSTICAL COLLINS, DR. The evaluation method selected was, as in Task II,\na hybrid approach: first, two clear-out screens were identified and applied to\nthe techniques. Lumped descriptors, each made up of many specific descriptors,\nwere then defined. The technologies applicable to each failure mode were graded\nusing these lumped descriptors, thus providing a ranking of the techniques. All\ntechniques were assumed to be equally developed for use on rocket engines. This\nappendix shows the technology grading performed for each failure mode. The scores\ngiven for technical lumped descriptors were summed to give an overall technical\nscore. Economic costs were subtracted from savings, resulting in an overall\nsavings figure. An economic grade was then assigned based on one point for each\nnearest $100,000 in savings. The development grade was determined by subtracting\none point, from a maximum of 10 points, for each year required for development.\nThus, three grades were obtained for each technology in each failure mode.\n\n287\n\nN\n\nBETWEEN-FLIGHT DIAGNOSTIC TECHNOLOGY GRADING\n\n00\n00\n\nFEATURES\n\nREQUIREMENTS\n\nCOOLANT PASSAGE\nLEAKAGE\n\nVI\n\n....\nVI\n\nL&.I\n\n0:\n0\n\n....\n....\n..J\n\nZ\n\n~\n\nSCANNING ACOUSTIC FLOW\nACOUSTIC HOLOGRAPHY\nX-RAY RADIOGRP,PHY\nGAMMA RADIOGRAPHY\nPENTOXIDE POLAROGRAPHY\n_ HYDROGEN POLAROGRAPHY\nHYGROMETRY\nSCANNING OPTICAL PYROMETRY\nHOLOGRAPHIC LEAK\nMILLIMETER~WAVE\n\ne\n.....\n\n....\n::5\n....\n\n>0:\n<:\n\n..J\n-J\n\n..J\n\n~\n.....\n\n.....\nce\n\n....\n\nI-\n\ne\n\n:t:\n\nz:\n\n....\n\nIII\n\n8\n\n~\n(!J\n\n20\n\n10\n\n20\n\n10\n\n100\n\n$OK\n\n12\n15\n8\n15\n12\n12\n10\n10\n8\n8\n\n7\n5\n5\n7\n4\n5\n5\n\n16\n18\n8\n8\n8\n12\n7\n12\n17\n12\n\n9\n9\n7\n3\n1\n8\n1\n9\n9\n8\n\n69\n-70\n38\n45\n45\n62\n43\n70\n65\n56\n\n200\n200\n100\n100\n200\n150\n50\n100\n200\n200\n\n20\n\n10\n\n10\n\n5\n\n6\n5\n2\n4\n6\n5\n6\n4\n4\n\ng\n....\n\n: ...\n\nC-\n\n8\n\n0\n\nC\n\n.....\n....\n\n...\n\n1\n2\n7\n5\n7\n5\n3\n3\n\n-J\n\nz:\n...\n....\n\n~\n\nz\n\n3\n\n....\nce\n\nVI\n\nVI\n\n<:\n\n14\n15\n7\n6\n7\n15\n7\n18\n17\n15\n\nVI\n\n:::l\n\n<:\n\nU\n.....\ntJ\no\n\nIt\'I\nlIl-\n\nu\n\n>....\n....\n\na:\n~\n\n....\nU\n\nu\n\n0\n.....\n.....\nc..\n\n-J\n\nc..\nc..\n\ne\n\n0:\n\n>-\n\n.....\n0:\n\nPERFECT SCORE\n\nVI\n\nVI\n\n....\nc..\nINSPFCTION\nTECHNOLOGY\n\nECONOMICAL\n\nTECHNICAL\n\nFAILURE MODE 2\n\n~\n0\n\n8\n\n7\n6\n\nu\n\n.....\n....\nz\n\n(!J\n\nz:\n\n;:\nc\n\n.
